Acknowledgements

Henrik Niemman and Niels Kjølstad Poulsen. They offered to us continuous help and guidance while
their suggestions and ideas gave us the needed motivation to solve the faced challenges and problems and

with us and the valuable insight she gave to us especially regarding the modelling of river systems.

gave helpful advices that inﬂuenced the outcome of our work. Last but not least, it has to be underlined that
the contribution of our colleagues at DTU was really important since numerous inspiring conversations took
place among us during the conduction of this master thesis.

==PAGE:9==
Abstract
This thesis covers the topic of modelling and control of a typical river system. The ﬁrst part of the project
concerns the modelling procedure and its target is to establish a simple, compact and accurate model of
a river reach. Water systems can be accurately described with the aid of the well known hydrodynamic
equations of de Saint Venant. As a matter of fact, the problem is that the strong non-linear character of these
equations leads to complex models that do not facilitate the design of controllers and therefore alternative
models should be sought. Howbeit, Saint Venant equations must be discretized in time and space with the aid
of numerical integration methods since they provide a trustworthy model that should be used as base in order
to evaluate other candidate models. A simpler model, already tested in numerous applications concerning
water systems, is the Integrator Delay (ID) model. As its name reveals, it simply consists of a time delay
for the water to travel from one upstream point of the reach to an other downstream (uniform ﬂow part)
together with an integrator at the end of the reach (backwater area). After the identiﬁcation of some crucial
parameters, it was found, by means of open and closed loop simulations, that ID model agrees with the Saint
Venant equations within the operating range and can, therefore, be the model used for control design.
As far as the control part is concerned, it is undeniable that a lot of different control methods have been
implemented successfully over the past years. Nevertheless, at this project Model Predictive Control (MPC)
was selected as the main control strategy. The reason behind this choice is the fact that MPC shows
some striking advantages. Shortly, its capability to incorporate the existing constraints of the system into
the formulation of the controller as well as the natural introduction of feedforward action by taking into
consideration the predicted disturbances, make MPC a rather appealing option. In order to see practically
the effect of the aforementioned advantages, various reference change and disturbance rejection tests were
performed where standard Linear Quadratic Regulator (LQR) was compared with MPC for a single and
a double reach river system. Actually, simulations veriﬁed that the system had a better behaviour when
MPC was used instead of LQR and as a consequence it was decided that MPC will be the selected control
methodology for the extended system. To be more speciﬁc, the extended river system treated at this thesis
consists of a river of four reaches and two lakes. One of them communicates with the river with the aid of a
pump and a turbine. In addition, through simulations it became evident that the implementation of MPC
often results to heavy computational load. Especially, slack variables, which were used in order to formulate
soft MPC, play a vital role in the increase of computational time. Henceforth, in order to face this serious
threat various ways to treat the slack variables were evaluated. Finally, the concept of Economic Model
Predictive Control (EMPC) was introduced. The potential of EMPC along with the changes it brings to
the formulation of the problem and the behaviour of the system are clearly shown with the help of suitable
simulations.

Preface
This thesis report documents the work and results of the project "Modelling and control of a river system"
conducted by Panagiotis Fykiris and Panagiotis Vavatsikos. This project represents a workload of 30 ECTS
points for each author and was carried out at Automation and Control, DTU Electrical Engineering in the
period from 26 January, 2015 to 26 June, 2015.
Panagiotis Fykiris (s131411)
Panagiotis Vavatsikos (s131365)
Kgs. Lyngby, June 26, 2015

Notation
Symbol
Description
y ∈Rn
A bold lower case symbol denotes a
vector of variables.
Y ∈Rn×m
A bold upper case symbol denotes a
matrix of variables.
[Y ]n,m
Element at the nth row and mth col-
umn of matrix Y .
yT
Transpose of vector y.
Y T
Transpose of matrix Y .
X ≥0
Positive semideﬁnite matrix X.
X > 0
Positive deﬁnite matrix X.
X−1
Inverse of matrix X.
xs
Stationary value of vector x.
X∞
Steady state version of time varying
matrix X(t) or X(k).
[X]n×m
Matrix X is stacked n times over the
vertical direction the and m times over
the horizontal direction.
diag{x}
Matrix where the diagonal elements
are equal to x and all the other equal
to zero.
dim(X)
The biggest dimension of matrix X.
∥y∥W
Weighted l2 norm of the vector y :
p
yT W y.

Zero matrix of size n × m.

the set of real n-tuples.

The rate of change of vector x.
n
X
t=0
a1(t), a2(t)
Sum of scalars a1, a2 from t = 0 until

∀t ∈[t0, t1]
For every t belonging to the set from
t0 to t1.
Xi,j
Matrix X where i refers to its name
and j refers to the system, j
∈
[sv, id, 1, 2, full] (Saint Venant, Inte-
grator Delay, Single Reach, Double
Reach and Full System respectively).
When there is only one subscript, it
refers to the system. Exception for the
second subscript is when it refers to
min or max.
xi,j
Variable x where i refers to its name
and j refers to its delayed time steps.

CHAPTER 1
General Introduction
1.1
Introduction and Motivation
While water becomes more and more expensive and rare source around the globe, few would deny that it
constitutes a vital aspect of human life. Actually, drinking, washing and growing crops are only some of the
most common uses of water since it is used also for generation of energy (hydroelectric), manufacturing
of a wide variety of products whilst it enables the use of ships and other water vehicles. Figure 1.1 depicts
the estimated water usage in the United States by 2005 by category. Therefore, water must be distributed
quickly and accurately in order to cover all these needs. For example, farmers use fresh water, which is
available in rivers or lakes, for irrigation purposes. Usually these sources of water are not close to the corps
and therefore canals should be created in order to connect the supply with the demand.
Figure 1.1: Estimated use of water in United States by 2005 [37].
From all the above, it becomes apparent that the establishment of organizations which manage the water
distribution in an area is indispensable. These organisations use control structures in order to regulate the
water ﬂows in the canals and serve all the existing needs. This delivery type is referred in the literature
as arranged delivery while on-demand delivery means that users can obtain the desired amount of water
without making a request in advance. It is certain that the last method is not so popular due to the scarcity of
1
==PAGE:32==
2
CHAPTER 1. GENERAL INTRODUCTION
water sources. Regarding arranged delivery, efﬁciency becomes a key factor since in practice a considerable
amount of water is wasted because of inaccurate or lack of control. For example in [47] it is reported that
according to calculations, 30% of the supplied water is wasted due to ineffective water distribution.
Figure 1.2: Number of ﬂoods worldwide, 1975- 2001 [51].
Figure 1.3: Number of deaths caused by ﬂoods worldwide, 1975-2001 [51].
On the other hand, of course, water can cause various dangerous situations that can even threaten human life
such as ﬂoods caused by huge river discharges or heavy precipitation. As reported in [12], ﬂoods consist the
most ubiquitous and costly natural hazard in the Unites States. As a matter of fact, the average damage from
ﬂoods is reported to be $5.2 billion and over 80 deaths per year. Furthermore, Figures 1.2 and 1.3 reveal
the severity of the danger that ﬂoods pose globally. Nevertheless, ﬂoods are not the only danger caused by
water. It is obvious that the rise of the world population together with the growth of the agricultural sector
lead to an increase in the demand of water and many reports, [34], predict that in the near future the water
resources will become extinct if no initiatives are taken in order to deal with this serious threat. Nowadays,
many governments and organisations around the globe invest time and money in order to develop techniques
that will allow a wiser exploitation of the available water resources and prepare for a drier future.
1.2
History of Water Management
By the term water management, it is meant that every water system should be treated in such a way to ensure
that:
==PAGE:33==
1.3. ESTABLISHMENT OF MODELS
3
• There is a balance between user interests.
• The available resources and equipment are used in an optimal manner.
• Environmental interests are respected.
• In case of an extreme situation, damages should be minimized.
As described in [76], looking back in time, the ﬁrst attempt to control the water was with mechanical
equipment, like ﬂoaters, which was attached to the gates of the rivers. As the effectiveness of these methods
was not satisfactory, the use of electrical automation came into play. The initial step was the use of automatic
water level sensors which recorded the level of water in multiple points. Afterwards, with the aid of
communication lines, it became possible to register the measurement from all the sensors in order to be
processed by a central computer as well as to send the calculated adjustments to the structures. As far as the
structures are concerned, electric motors and automated valves were added in order to perform the ordered
actions more accurately. Howbeit, due to the fact that the required funds were usually hard to be raised
and the communication lines tended to fail very often, many water authorities seemed to prefer the local
control by using Programmable Logic Controllers (PLC) or even manual regulation. The development of
more trustworthy communication lines together with the low price of computational power allowed the
implementation of more advanced water management strategies that will consist one of the basic topics of
this thesis. Note also that, nowadays, in the majority of the cases, the procedure of measuring, computing
the control actions and adjusting the structures is fully automated.
1.3
Establishment of Models
Modelling is always a very important step before designing a trustworthy control system. It is sure that
a valid model is needed not only in order to develop the desired control strategies but also for simulation
purposes. Simulations are vital as, ﬁrstly and foremost, there is a very limited access to the physical system
in order to perform the required experiments. In addition, simulations facilitate the addition and the removal
of infrastructures to the model and consequently the effect they have can be investigated thoroughly. Last but
not least, simulation models allow the creation and the execution of a grand variety of scenarios in order to
evaluate the performance of the controller. Undoubtedly, these models should be simple and demand small
computational times while they capture all the essential dynamics. Moreover, there is a clear preference for
linear models since control theory for this type of systems is rich and well established.
Generally speaking, when it comes to modelling, two are the available approaches; physical and empirical.
A crucial assumption in order to use empirical modelling is that real data are available and with the aid
of system identiﬁcation methods can give a reliable and simple model of the system under investigation.
The acquired model is called black box model as it does not provide any physical insight to the system.
Oppositely, with physical modelling, physical laws that govern the system are exploited in order to obtain
a valid model. For water systems, Saint-Venant equations are often used as a starting point. Despite the
fact that physical models give a very deep knowledge of the system, they usually lead to non-linear models
and heavy computational burden whereas there is an inevitable need for estimation of some parameters.
Therefore, it is apparent that investigation is needed in order to ﬁnd a model which keeps the right balance
between accuracy and computational times.
==PAGE:34==
4
CHAPTER 1. GENERAL INTRODUCTION
As there is no available data from a speciﬁc river, the benchmark model of [62] was used as a basis. The
model is depicted in Figure 1.4.
Figure 1.4: The benchmark model [61].
The benchmark model is basically composed of 2 lakes, L1 and L2, and a river which is divided in 6 reaches
(R1, R2, R3, R4, R5 and R6). Apart from that, the lakes and the river reaches are connected with ducts
equipped with either only turbines (T1 and T2) or pumps and turbines (C1 and C2). The river is fed by
the ﬂows qin and qtributary and the reaches are terminated with dams equipped with turbines for power
production (D1, D2 ,D3, D4, D5 and D6). It goes without saying that all these components need to be
modelled in order to obtain a trustworthy model suitable for simulations. Note that the benchmark model
was used as a basis of inspiration and some alterations, which will be pointed out when this is the case, were
performed.
1.4
Control Methods and Objectives
In [13] the used controllers for water systems are categorized as follows : ﬂow, volume and water levels
controllers. With the ﬁrst type of controller a desired ﬂow is imposed at a structure while with the volume
controller a target value for the volume of water in a reach is achieved. Finally, when water level control is
used, the goal is to obtain the desired values for the height of water at the end of the reaches. Note that the
last type of controllers will be the case for this thesis.
The real time management of an open-channel irrigation canal is a difﬁcult task taking into consideration
that there is a time delay between the water resource (upstream level) and the water user (downstream level).
In [61] and [42] two canal operation concepts are presented; supply oriented and demand oriented operation.
During the ﬁrst one, the upstream water supply inﬂow determines the ﬂow schedule and is mainly used in
the majority of irrigated perimeters managed with a scheduled rotational delivery (strict delivery schedule
set by the authorities). In demand oriented operation, the demanded off-takes determine the ﬂow schedule
and obviously the inﬂow is decided by the canal manager in order to meet these demands. Regarding the
establishment of the control concept, two main categories can be identiﬁed; upstream and downstream
control that are connected with supply and demand oriented operation, respectively.
==PAGE:35==
1.4. CONTROL METHODS AND OBJECTIVES
5
In point of fact, a simple way to cover the water needs would be to deliver a constant upstream discharge
corresponding to the maximal water demand. This kind of water management is known as upstream control.
This strategy is inefﬁcient with demand oriented operation as there is no way to adapt the ﬂow at the head
in order to meet the demanded off-takes. Demanded off-takes often change as crops have unpredictable
water requirements that change in the course of time, rain falls unpredictably in general and may decrease
or increase substantially the demand of water and farmers may desire to irrigate during speciﬁc time slots
in order to exploit lower prices of electricity for their pumps, to name some reasons. Therefore, upstream
control is considered the best choice for drainage systems and is avoided for irrigation canals.
Oppositely, downstream control represents the strategy where the off-takers are the ones who determine the
schedule of the canal system and consequently is suitable for irrigation canals. This control strategy cannot
immediately satisfy the water demands as there is a time delay between the upstream water level (source)
and the downstream (consumers). This strategy is totally incompatible with supply oriented operation as the
upstream ﬂow cannot be ﬁxed in advance but has to be a consequence of the downstream demand.
Figure 1.5: Upstream control scheme [61].
Figure 1.6: Downstream control scheme [61].
==PAGE:36==
6
CHAPTER 1. GENERAL INTRODUCTION
To sum up and taking into account the physical limitations two are the main ways to control an irrigation
canal [44]:
1. Distant downstream control, as shown in Figure 1.6, where the upstream control variable (inﬂow) is
manipulated in order to regulate the water level located at the downstream end.
2. Local upstream control, as shown in Figure 1.5 where the downstream control variable (outﬂow) is
manipulated in order to regulate an upstream water level.
Moreover, the level from which information are collected in order to calculate the control action, categorizes
the control methods to central and local (decentralised). More speciﬁcally, central control means that there
is only one controller which collects information from all the measurement points and sends the suitable
actions to actuators that are in charge of moving the structures at remote locations. Undoubtedly, a controller
for the whole system can achieve better performance than multiple local controllers as it takes care of all
the interconnections between the different subsystems. Thereby, if a control action is regarded optimal, in
some sense, for a subsystem but not for the total system, it will not be executed. Nevertheless, this comes at
the price of having harder optimization problems to solve when optimal controllers are used. Furthermore,
subsystems are usually located far from each other and the communication lines between them can easily
fail leading to the failure of the controller with, maybe, devastating results.
Oppositely, with decentralised control, every subsystem is regulated independently from each other without
taking into consideration the way it will affect the other subsystems. Despite the fact that there is no threat of
communication failure as the measurements are executed locally, controlling every subsystem independently
can damage the overall system. That means that each controller is tuned in order to achieve high performance
for the speciﬁc subsystem without considering that these actions will affect the rest of the system. The most
frequent problem is the disturbance ampliﬁcation that can even lead to instability. In [78], this problem was
faced by using a cost function that was taking into consideration the performance of all the subsystems. In
addition, in [28], where power plants in a river are treated, centralized Model Predictive Control (MPC)
seems to deliver better results than decentralised PI controllers. Regarding this thesis, centralised control
schemes will be considered.
Taking all the above into consideration, the main objective of control is to guarantee outﬂows requested
by users while maintaining the level of water close to the set point values. The controlled variables are,
therefore, these water heights. Veritably, water levels are used very often from operators in order to deﬁne
the desired state of a water system. The cause behind this choice is that water levels exhibit slow dynamics
whereas they can easily be measured. Apart from that, it is declarative that water levels are included in
almost every formula that concerns water management. Furthermore, the manipulated variables are usually
the openings of the gates. However, as it will be explained later, it is preferable to consider the ﬂow as a
manipulated variable and then use a local controller to adjust the gate opening. Additionally, the off-take
outﬂows from users and inﬂows from rainfalls act as disturbances for the water system. Note that these
disturbances can be measured, predicted or estimated. Last but not least, the system has some constraints
that cannot be avoided. Typical examples of these constraints are the maximum and minimum water levels
along the canal and the maximum and minimum ﬂows through the gates or the pumps.
Actually, there are many available control strategies, starting from feedback and feedforward controllers and
extend till heuristic methods and optimal controllers, and most of them have been successfully implemented .
==PAGE:37==
1.5. PROBLEM STATEMENT AND SCOPE OF THE PROJECT
7
1.5
Problem Statement and Scope of the Project
As it has been already mentioned, the main motivating factor behind this thesis is to ameliorate the sustain-
ability of water resources. Hence, the main scope of this thesis is to examine and develop strategies for
accurate modelling and efﬁcient control of river systems. To be more speciﬁc, by accuracy of the modelling
it is meant that the model should be able to capture all the essential dynamics of the river whereas it should
be simple enough for control purposes. Therefore, one main target of the thesis is to obtain a model with
these features.
Furthermore, efﬁciency of the control implies that it should ensure that water wastage is minimum whilst
the water demands are satisﬁed and various environmental regulations are respected. The main challenge
behind this is the fact that the river should meet multiple and sometimes even contradictory speciﬁcations.
For example, it has already been mentioned that the water may be used for farming, urban and industrial
usages while an adequate amount of water should always remain in the river for environmental (for instance
the ﬂow should not be less than a deﬁned value in order to preserve the ﬂora and fauna of the river) and
recreational purposes. Additionally, it should not be forgotten that the controller should be able to prevent or
to limit the effect of ﬂoods. Moreover, an important aspect that should be addressed is the most efﬁcient use
of the available (or possibly available) infrastructures by the controller.
Apart from that, the developed controller should be able to use effectively the knowledge about the distur-
bances. For example, given that the farmers make their orders in advance, the controller should be able to
store more water in the canals before the offtakes happen. Additionally, when extreme storm events are
predicted, the controller should have the capability to start lowering the water levels some hours before
the storm event actually takes place in order to prevent ﬂoods. Finally, the controller should be easy to
implement, demand small computational times and must have the ability to handle the existing constraints of
the system.
To achieve all the aforementioned goals with a control system, a careful choice of controller should be
made. From the control methods listed before, MPC, which belongs to the class of optimal controllers,
seems the most suitable mainly because of its ability to handle constraints and the combination of feedback
action on measured water levels and feedforward action on the predicted disturbances. Moreover, MPC
seems to be able to cope with both set-point and ﬂood control. Therefore, besides developing MPC strategy,
scope of this project is to prove its superiority over other control methods and its ability to meet all the
desired speciﬁcations while trying to reduce the computational load that often constitutes a challenging
aspect regarding the use of MPC. Apart from that, with appropriate manipulations, the scheme of standard
MPC can be transformed to this of EMPC where the main target is to minimize the cost of operation (or to
maximize the proﬁt) of the system. So, the ﬁnal aim of this project will be to implement EMPC and illustrate
its potential when it comes to river systems.
1.6
Outline of the Thesis
The thesis is structured according to the following way:
• Chapter 2 presents the dynamic behaviour of open water systems and analyses the most frequently
used models. Furthermore, an introduction to some common hydraulic structures is given.
==PAGE:38==
8
CHAPTER 1. GENERAL INTRODUCTION
• Chapter 3 provides a presentation of all the available control methods for typical river systems whilst
Linear Quadratic Regulator (LQR) and MPC are developed in full detail.
• Chapter 4 deals with the establishment of a valid and simple model for a river reach. Open and closed
loop simulations are performed in order to compare and evaluate the obtained models.
• Chapter 5 includes the design of MPC and LQR for simple water systems consisting of one and two
reaches and a comparison between these two control methods is made.
• Chapter 6 demonstrates the full water system under examination. Suitable simulations are conducted
in order to compare and evaluate ways to reduce the computational load of MPC.
• Chapter 7 introduces the basic notion of economic MPC and with the aid of simulations its core
advantages become clear.
• Chapter 8 contains the conclusions made out of this thesis and proposals for future work.
• Appendix consists of three parts. The ﬁrst part presents some supportive theoretical parts whereas the
second one includes the state space representation of all the models that are used. Finally, the last part
of the Appendix is employed for exhibition of additional plots and simulation scenarios.
Note that all the parameters of the simulated river system and the tuning parameters of the used controllers
are provided in detail in order to be possible to reproduce the obtained results.
==PAGE:39==
CHAPTER 2
Modelling of Water Systems
2.1
Introduction
This chapter deals with the presentation of all the available options concerning the modelling of water
systems. Firstly, the full hydrodynamic Saint Venant equations will be presented together with the different
resistance laws that may be selected. Afterwards, due to the fact that Saint Venant equations do not have an
analytical solution, numerical methods are needed and will be examined in order to ﬁnd the most suitable
one. In addition, as the mathematical models usually have a high level of complexity, it is prudent to search
for simpler models that are able to maintain the essential dynamics of a river system needed for control
purposes. Integrator Delay (ID) model seems to be a strong candidate and thereby it will be examined in
detail. Certainly, other methods of river modelling also exist like system identiﬁcation. The key idea behind
this strategy is the use of input-output data in order to build a mathematical model that describes accurately
the dynamical model under investigation. However, as fas as this thesis is concerned, system identiﬁcation
tactics cannot be applied as operational data of a real river are not available. The last topic treated by this
chapter concerns the hydraulic structures used in order to control water ﬂows in river systems like gates,
turbines, pumps and weirs.
2.2
Derivation of Saint Venant Equations
Saint Venant equations are frequently used to model the dynamics of water levels and discharges of river
reaches. The one-dimensional partial differential Saint Venant equations are [41]:

The two aforementioned equations describe the mass and momentum balance while:
• x represents the spatial variable, [m].
• t represents the time variable, [s].
• q is the ﬂow of the river, [m3/s].
• h is the level of the water with respect to the river bed, [m].

==PAGE:40==

CHAPTER 2. MODELLING OF WATER SYSTEMS
• A represents the wetted area (see Figure 2.3), [m2].
• If is the friction slope, [−].
• I0 is the river bed slope, [−].
• g is the gravitational acceleration, [m/s2].
• ql is the lateral inﬂow per space unit, [m3/s].
The momentum balance (see second equation in 2.1) consists of a summation of the contribution of the
inertia (1), the advection (2), the gravitational force (3) and frictional force (4). The Saint Venant equations
hold true under some speciﬁc assumptions [16]:
• The vertical distribution of the pressure is hydrostatic. This assumption is considered well founded as
long as the streamlines do not have sharp curvatures.
• The channel bottom slope (the angle between the river bed and the reference datum) is small. This
means that the ﬂow depth measured normal to the channel bottom or measured vertically is approxi-
mately the same.
• The channel cross section and channel bottom slope do not change with distance.
• The ﬂow velocity is assumed to be uniform along a channel section.
• The ﬂow is incompressible and one-dimensional. This means that the water surface across all the
cross sections is horizontal.
• The head losses in unsteady ﬂow can be simulated by using the steady state resistance laws that will
be examined later. In other words, head losses (friction losses and losses due to the channel expansion
and contraction which decrease the mechanical energy of the system) during unsteady ﬂow condition
can be assumed to be approximately equal to these during steady ﬂow condition.
The next section examines the way Saint Venant equations can be derived using some simple laws of
conservation and is mainly inspired by [49].
2.2.1
Conservation of Mass
The ﬁrst Saint-Venant equation or continuity equation represents the law of mass balance. Figure 2.1 depicts
a volume element of an open channel between an upstream level, U, and a downstream level, D. The average
cross-sectional area is A (is also named wetted area as seen before) and ∆x is the length of the element
along the ﬂow direction. It is certain that:
Rate of mass transfer = ρq
(2.2)
where ρ is the density, [kg/m3], and q is a discharge ﬂow. Rate of mass transfer is substantially the rate with
which the mass is transformed through a channel section. Observing that the water enters section U with a

2.2.2
Conservation of Momentum
The second Saint-Venant equation is obtained using the law of conservation of momentum and is also called
dynamic equation. Figure 2.2 presents again a volume element of an open channel, like in Figure 2.1, where
V is the ﬂow velocity, [m, /s], Fp is the pressure force, [N], Ff is the friction force, [N] and W is the force
due to the weight of water, [N]. It is certain that the rate of change of momentum within the volume element
equals the net rate of momentum transfer into the volume element plus the sum of all the forces acting on
the volume element. Furthermore, it holds true that:
Rate of momentum transfer = βρqV

2.2.3
Restrictions of Saint Venant Equations
It has already been said that Saint-Venant equations are only valid when some speciﬁc assumptions hold
true. As discussed in [41] the following limitations are more often encountered with respect to Saint-Venant
equations:
• Basically, one crucial assumption that goes with the Saint-Venant is that the ﬂow is one-dimensional.
Nevertheless, this is not always the case. For example, in ﬂood plains or in large rivers, two-
dimensional Saint-Venant equations provide more accuracy. In this thesis, only ﬂow patterns that can
be accurately modelled with the one-dimensional Saint-Venant equations are treated. Fortunately, this
is true for the vast majority of open channels which are controlled with hydraulic structures
• As it has been explained, Saint-Venant equations assume that the pressure distribution of the liquid
is hydrostatic. When this does not hold true, an accurate model can be obtained using Boussinesq
equations [35].
• While weirs and gates are the most commonly encountered hydraulic structures related to control
of open channels, it is certain that they result to sharp discontinuities and as a result Saint-Venant
equations stop to be valid. However, Bernoulli theorem offers a static algebraic relationship between
the ﬂow variables and allows to consider the aforementioned hydraulic structures as internal boundaries.
The most common form of Bernoulli theorem concerns the steady ﬂow of an incompressible ﬂuid and
is given by [41]:
p
ρ + V 2
2 + gh = constant
(2.21)
where p is the pressure of the ﬂuid, [Nm−2].
2.2.4
Numerical Solution Methods
The just presented Saint-Venant equations allow to calculate the partial derivatives of the dependent variables;
ﬂow, q, and water levels with respect to the river bed, h. Nevertheless, the practical value is small as it would
be preferable to know the exact values of these variables. Therefore, it goes without saying that Saint-Venant
equations should be integrated. Unfortunately, due to the strong non-linear character of these equations, it is
==PAGE:45==
2.2. DERIVATION OF SAINT VENANT EQUATIONS
15
not possible to acquire a close-form solution and so only numerical integration can offer a solution. Some of
the most popular numerical methods are [16]:
• Finite-difference method (FDM): The partial derivatives are calculated with the help of algebraic
relations at some particular points or nodes [71]. As this is a very popular method, it will be presented
later in detail.
• Method of characteristics: This method suggests the discovery of curves, which are called charac-
teristic, along which the partial differential equations (PDEs) become ordinary differential equations
(ODEs).
• Method of lines (MoL): The discretization takes place only along one dimension whereas an analytical
solution is used in the remaining direction [60]. Since this is the selected method for this thesis, it will
also be demonstrated in detail.
• Finite-element method: The computing domain is divided into elements like triangles and afterwards
nodes, where the numerical solutions are determined, are placed within each element [36].
• Finite-volume method: The ﬁrst step is the formulation of a discretization using an integral form of
the equations followed by the subdivision of the computing domain into ﬁnite volumes. The major
disadvantage of this method is the fact that it requires geometrical information concerning the model
and subsequently leads to a high computational load. This problem applies also to the ﬁnite-element
method and henceforth they are used only for complex problems [38].
• Spectral method: The basic idea is to express the solution of the differential equation as the sum of
some basic functions (like Fast Fourier Transformation (FFT)). This method is reported to offer bigger
accuracy compared to ﬁnite-difference and ﬁnite-element methods [18].
• Boundary-element method: This method is used when the PDEs have been formulated as integral
equations. The core idea is to use the given boundary conditions to ﬁt boundary values into the integral
form. Under some circumstances, this strategy is more efﬁcient than ﬁnite-element method [3].
Finite Difference Method
Following the analysis of [15] and [7], it is clear that the FDM works by replacing the region over which the
independent variables in the PDEs are deﬁned, by a ﬁnite grid of points at which the dependent variable is
approximated using Taylor’s theorem. Hence, if f(x) has n continuous derivatives over the interval (a, b)
then for a < x0, x0 + ∆x < b:

Method of Lines
Based on [48], the MoL was chosen in this thesis in order to deal with the partial differential Saint Venant
equations. As it has already been demonstrated, the FD schemes involve the discretization in both space and
time. Oppositely, the MoL suggests the discretization of either space or time and the continuous solution
along the other dimension. Again, the PDEs are transformed into ODEs. As stated in [60] this method has a
number of strong features:
==PAGE:49==
2.2. DERIVATION OF SAINT VENANT EQUATIONS
19
• Due to the semianalytical approach of this method, accurate results are obtained with less computa-
tional effort compared to FD method.
• By discretizing in space and time separately, it is easy to guarantee stability and convergence.
• Well found and trustworthy ODEs solvers can be used and because of the fact that only a small amount
of discretization lines is usually needed, a limited set of equations has to be solved.
The normal procedure suggested by the method of lines includes the following steps [60]:
1. Selection of the solution region and afterwards partitioning into sectors. It should be remembered that
a more dense partition leads to a more accurate solution although this is not always the case as it will
be discussed later.
2. Discretization of the PDEs in one direction.
3. Transformation in order to acquire ODEs.
4. Introduction of existing boundary conditions and solution of the equations.
To better illustrate the concept of the method of lines, let us consider the 1-d linear advection equation [81]:
∂u
∂t = −υ ∂u
∂x
(2.30)
where υ is the velocity, [m/s], which is considered constant. Essentially, this equation describes passive
advection of a scalar ﬁeld u(x, t) carried along by a ﬂow of constant speed υ. Assuming that Equation 2.30
is approximated in a spatial grid of x using N points whose distance from each other is constant and equal
to ∆x, then using the CFD approximations, described by Equation 2.28, it is straightforward to write:
∂u

Note that u0 and uN+1 constitute ﬁctitious points as they are outside the spatial domain and thereby should
be included in the ordinary differential equations for i = 1 and i = N. As analysed in [81] the solution
obtained after these steps often suffers from numerical oscillations. Despite the fact that ﬁnite difference
approximation is second order accurate, which implies that the accuracy should be increased while N is
increased, in practice it does not seem that a decreased ∆x leads necessarily to less oscillations. Thereafter,
it becomes clear that a lot of consideration should be given regarding the implementation of approximations
in order to end up with a stable and accurate solution. A suggested way to overcome this serious problem is
to use another method for spatial discretization. In [81], the ﬁrst order, two point upwind FD approximation
is proposed, where:

2.3
Integrator Delay Model
As it was explained previously, the Saint-Venant equations model the water ﬂows very accurately and capture
all the relevant dynamics when the discretization is performed with a small grid size ∆x and a small time
step ∆t. Moreover, the obtained results should be calibrated and validated by means of actual measurements.
As it will be proven later, the model is very accurate as it captures all the essential dynamics especially
around the linearization point but leads to heavy computational times and thereby the need of a simpliﬁed
model for control becomes vital.
Among other approaches, in order to simplify the partial differential Saint-Venant equations, the Integrator-
delay (ID) model plays an important role and has been successfully employed in numerous control application
concerning water systems [9], [11],[64], [65], [76]. The underlying assumption behind this model is that
probably the most common ﬂow proﬁle regarding open channels is the backwater curve, [20], as depicted in
Figure 2.8. Evidently, the ID model divides the channel into two parts: a part where the ﬂow is uniform
and a part that is affected by backwater. By observing Figure 2.8, it is easy to notice that the water depth at
the downstream level is bigger than the normal depth while it is decreasing going upwards until it either
becomes equal to the normal depth or reaches the upstream end of the reach. However, the assumption of
uniform ﬂow is rarely the case as various factors like geometrical irregularities of the bed slope, result to a
changing water depth over distance. The solution is to assume that if the depth of the ﬂow is close to the
normal depth, the ﬂow can be considered uniform, [64], [10].
Figure 2.8: Backwater curve for ID model [10].
==PAGE:51==
2.3. INTEGRATOR DELAY MODEL
21
Initially, the water level at the backwater area is assumed to be horizontal. A second crucial assumption is
that the time delay is connected with the part of the uniform ﬂow when the water travels from the upstream
point to the downstream one. In particular, the cause of this time delay is mainly the friction forces as
revealed from Saint Venant equations (see Equation 2.1). Thereby, at the part of the uniform ﬂow, where the
velocity of water is higher, the friction plays a more crucial role than in the backwater area. Apart from that,
a key notion is that the backwater area is substantially a water volume integrator of all the existing deviations
of the ﬂows in it. An additional important assumption is that all the lateral inﬂows or outﬂows are located at
the backwater area, [82].
Figure 2.9: Integrator-delay simpliﬁed model [61].
As ID model simply consists of a time delay and an integrator part and exploiting the law of
conservation of volume, the following equation is obtained:
As · (h(k + 1) −h(k)) = Ts · (qin(k −kd) + qinlat(k) −qout(k) −qlat(k))
(2.35)
referring to Figure 2.9, where:
• Ts is the sampling time, [s].
• qin(k) is the inﬂow at the upstream level, [m3/s].
• qout(k) is the outﬂow at the downstram level, [m3/s]
• qinlat(k) is a lateral inﬂow, for example rainfall or another small river, located at the backwater area,
[m3/s].
• qlat(k) is a lateral off-take, such as customers orders, located at the backwater area, [m3/s].
• As is the backwater area,

m2
.
• kd is the number of delay time steps.
==PAGE:52==
22
CHAPTER 2. MODELLING OF WATER SYSTEMS
As described in [40], if the transfer function from upstream inﬂow to the water level at the end of reach is
denoted as G1(s) and respectively from downstream outﬂow as G2(s), a canal reach can be represented by
the following relation:
h(s) = G1(s)qin(s) + G2(s)(qout(s) + qlat(s) −qinlat(s))
(2.36)
where:
G1(s) = e−kds
Ass ,
G2(s) = −1
Ass
(2.37)
Substantially, the main advantage of this model is its simplicity and compactness that lead to signiﬁcantly
small computational time. The drawback is that the values of the parameters (time delay and backwater area)
are valid only at a range of operating points. This means that the values of these parameters change when the
ﬂow changes as will be described later in Section 2.3.1. Moreover, it should be examined how important are
the dynamics neglected by the ID model before using it as a basis for control design.
It should be highlighted that in some cases, especially for short oscillating canals, a zero should be added
to the ID model in order to establish the Integrator Delay Zero (IDZ) model [39]. This model is able to
represent accurately the open channel behaviour both in low and high frequencies. To be more speciﬁc,
the ID model accounts for the low frequencies band whilst the added zero models the direct inﬂuence of
the discharge on the water level at high frequencies. Thereafter, the IDZ model provides not only accurate
approximations for time delay and backwater area for the classical ID model but also a better match in higher
frequencies due to the added zero. Even if it is more accurate, this model will not be examined during this
thesis as there is the assumption that the operation of the system is conducted in low frequencies.
2.3.1
Flow Conditions Problem
It is well known from [42] and [72] that ﬂow conditions affect noticeably the time delay of a river system
and of course play an important role regarding the robustness margins of the designed control system. As a
matter of fact, in [26], it is proven that the time delay changes substantially with the ﬂow conditions. More
speciﬁcally, a larger time delay is obtained for lower ﬂow conditions and a smaller time delay is the case for
the higher ﬂow conditions. This is fairly reasonable thinking that with higher ﬂow, the water travels faster
through the uniform ﬂow part. However, it should be mentioned that a higher ﬂow means a longer part of
uniform ﬂow (as a result of the increased water level) and a shorter backwater area. Actually, the effect
of this increase in the length of the uniform ﬂow part is relatively small compared to this of the increase
of the velocity of the ﬂow and therefore a smaller time delay is obtained, most of the times. As far as the
backwater area is concerned, the effect of a higher ﬂow depends on the type of the cross sectional area since
the backwater area is calculated using the length as well as the width of the backwater part of the reach.
Finally, in [26] frequency tests are conducted for the different ﬂow conditions and the conclusion is that
the low ﬂow condition is the most challenging one and thereby any robust controller designed for low ﬂow
conditions should be appropriate also for medium and high ﬂow patterns. Apparently, when a controller is
designed for low ﬂow conditions a trade-off between performance and robustness is achieved.
==PAGE:53==
2.4. RESERVOIR MODEL
23
2.4
Reservoir Model
As described in [7] and [10], an alternative to ID model is used; the reservoir model. As stated from the
authors, this model has some important advantages since it is accurate enough and not computationally heavy
at the same time. The most severe drawback is that the time delay needed from the water to be transferred
from one end to the other, is not taken into account in this model and this can cause problems during the
modelling of large reservoirs. The ﬁrst step in order to create a reservoir model is to divide the reach into a
number of separate subcatchments. These, subcatchments form different reservoir models, while all together
connected can approximate the model of a river reach. In each of these reservoir models, the basic laws of
conservation of mass and volume hold true. Therefore, if each reservoir is considered to be a big tank of
water, the change of the volume in it is connected to the amount of water entering and leaving from it with
the following simple relation:
dVl(t)
dt
= qin(t) −qout(t)
(2.38)
where Vl represents the volume of water in the reservoir, [m3], qin represents the sum of inﬂow discharges,
[m3/s] and qout represents the sum of outﬂow discharges, [m3/s]. Discretization of the process in space
usually takes place only at locations which are of special interest, like upstream and downstream of hydraulic
structures together with locations along the river where ﬂoods may become a severe threat. Frequently,
it is of particular interest to know the relation between the volume of the water in the reservoir and the
corresponding water level, hres. The shape of the reservoir is a leading factor and the relation is, for the
majority of the cases, nonlinear. An example of a reservoir with two inﬂows, one of which is controlled by a
gate, and an outﬂow can be seen in Figure 2.10.
Figure 2.10: Example of a reservoir [10].
More details about the different reservoir models, their advantages and disadvantages as well as detailed
formulas can be found in [74]. Regarding this thesis, Equation 2.38 will be used in order to model lakes that
interact with the river under investigation.
2.5
Additional Parameters Regarding Modelling
It is unquestionable that during the modelling process many factors are disregarded so far, for the sake of
simplicity. First of all, interactions between the ground and the river have not been considered. In fact, very
often reaches gain or lose water from ground water and if it is necessary they can be modelled as constant
lateral intakes or off-takes. In addition, often evaporation, which is not treated in the thesis, can often be
signiﬁcant and act as a disturbance to the system. When this is the case, it is important to formulate a model
for evaporation in order to relate the surface area and the evaporation. Afterwards, this information can
==PAGE:54==
24
CHAPTER 2. MODELLING OF WATER SYSTEMS
be part of a controller with feedforward action, like MPC. Note that evaporation generally depends on the
temperature, the humidity of the air and ﬁnally the solar radiation. Apart from that, rainfall and small rivers
contribution can be modelled as disturbances acting on the river system and this will be the case for this
thesis.
2.6
Hydraulic Structures
At this point, some essential modelling aspects of various hydraulic structures that will be employed later,
will be discussed.
2.6.1
Gate Structures
It is true that in the vast majority of the cases, structures are used to manipulate the water ﬂows in a water
system. Actually, the most common ones are the overshot and undershot gates [61]. As a matter of fact, the
overshot gate backs up water as the water has to ﬂow over the crest of the gate. On the other hand, undershot
gate is put into the water from the top and the water ﬂows under it. One of the dominant suggested formulas
to model an overshot gate, which is depicted in Figure 2.11, is:
qgate = Cd · Lgate ·
r
2
3 · g · (h1 −hcr)(3/2)
(2.39)
where Lgate is the width of gate, [m], Cd is a discharge coefﬁcient, [−], g is the gravitational acceleration,
[m/s2], qgate is the discharge through the gate, [m3/s], h1 the upstream water level, [m], and hcr is the crest
level, [m].
Figure 2.11: Overshot gate [61].
Obviously, the ﬂow can be reduced by simply raising the gate. From Equation 2.39 it becomes clear that due
to the existence of the power of 3
2, if the upstream level is increased (due to a disturbance for example), the
ﬂow will increase more than proportional in order to bring the water level down again (feedback action).
Regarding the undershot gates now, the ﬂow through them can be either free or submerged. Free ﬂow means
that the downstream water level is lower than the bottom of the gate and has no inﬂuence on the discharge
through the gate whilst in submerged ﬂow it is higher and has an effect on the discharge. In Figures 2.40 and
2.41 these two types are presented. The corresponding formulas for the undershot gates are:
qgate = Cd · Lgate · l ·
p

where l is the gate opening , [m] and h2 is the downstream water level, [m]. Generally speaking, undershot
gates are more suitable for controlling downstream water levels than upstream. In this case, by altering the
gate height, the ﬂow can be regulated more accurately compared to an overshot gate.
Figure 2.12: Undershot gate for free ﬂow conditions [61].
Figure 2.13: Undershot gate for submerged ﬂow conditions [61].
It should be highlighted that this thesis will not deal further with gates. As it will be explained later in detail,
the calculated control action will be the desired ﬂow and afterwards, it is supposed that a local controller
will adjust the height of the gate accordingly in order to achieve the ﬂow ordered by the controller.
2.6.2
Weirs
As a matter of fact, a weir constitutes a barrier across a river whose aim is to alter the ﬂow characteristics.
The edge of surface over which the water ﬂows is named crest of the weir whereas a weir with a sharp
upstream corner is usually called sharp-crested weir [26]. The basic rule of operation of weirs can be
described by a basic rule; if the upstream water level is smaller or equal to the height of the weir crest then
the ﬂow over the weir is zero whilst when the upstream water level is higher than the height of the weir crest,
the ﬂow is given by the following formula [26] :
q(t) = cweir(h(t) −b)
3
2
(2.42)
==PAGE:56==
26
CHAPTER 2. MODELLING OF WATER SYSTEMS
where b is the height of the weir, [m]. Moreover, it is true that [8]:
cweir ≃0.6√gwweir
(2.43)
with wweir being the width of the crest, [m]. A schematic representation of a weir can be seen in Figure
2.14.
Figure 2.14: Sharp crester weir [26].
2.6.3
Pumps
A pump is actually an electro-mechanical structure which is used in order to transfer a liquid from one place
to another. Thereby, when it comes to water systems, pumps are mainly used in order to remove water
from a river, lake or a reservoir or to circulate water through a distribution system. In addition, the basic
components of a pumping station are [67]:
• The building that hosts the pumps and constitutes a pumping station.
• The hydraulic system that contains the pipes and the pump with the rotating blades or a screw propeller.
• The electrical system that contains the motor.
• The control system with the measurement devices of ﬂow and pressure.
There are two main categories of pumps. The ﬁrst one, called dynamic pumps, can be used when high
volumes of water are requested and the change in ﬂow is not a problem. Oppositely, displacement pumps
are employed when small but precise volumes of water are requested. Probably, the most common type of
pump that is used in water systems is the centrifugal one that belongs to the ﬁrst category. A schematic
representation can be seen in Figure 2.15. Shortly, an impeller (rotating set of vanes), which is placed
inside a volute (shival-shaped case), puts pressure in the water by accelerating its ﬂuid particles using the
centrifugal force [29].
The power absorbed by a pump can be calculated using the following formula [62]:
Pc(t) = qc(t)kc∆hc(t)
(2.44)
where kc is the pump coefﬁcient, [Jm−4], and ∆hc(t) is the pump head, [m]. Head represents how high can
the water be pushed. Assuming that water is pumped from a surface with a water level, hlv1(t), to a surface
==PAGE:57==
2.6. HYDRAULIC STRUCTURES
27
Figure 2.15: Centrifugal pump [24].
with water level, hlv2(t) and that the height difference of the duct of the pump is denoted as ∆hP , then the
head can be calculated using the expression:
∆hc(t) = ∆hC + hlv1(t) −hlv2(t)
(2.45)
Oppositely to the ﬁrst simple pumps that could only be turned on or off, modern pumps use electronic drivers
that allow operation at various ﬂows. In particular, local ﬂow controllers can be employed in order to ensure
any ﬂow between zero and the maximum constraint. Ultimately, in river systems, it is common practice to
use multiple pumps, creating a pump station, in order to be able to move larger amounts of water faster. One
of the biggest pumping stations in Europe, nowadays, is IJmuiden, located at the Netherlands, with a design
ﬂow of 260 m3/s.
2.6.4
Turbines
Turbines convert the energy that contains the water under pressure into mechanical energy. Afterwards, with
the aid of a generator (synchronous, induction or DC) this mechanical energy can be converted to electrical
energy. There are mainly two categories of turbines; impulse and reaction [73]. Both types can be seen in
Figure 2.16. Impulse turbines, use the kinetic energy of water in order to rotate its blades. The used water
falls to the bottom of the turbine house and just ﬂows out. This type of turbines is usually used with high
hydraulic head (water falls from a large height) and offer reliability, low maintenance cost and over 90%
efﬁciency [46]. On the other hand, the second category, which is actually the most widely used, refers to
turbines that use vertical or horizontal wheel that is fully submerged. The water changes its pressure moving
through the turbine and gives its energy.
The most common types of reaction turbines used for hydro power generation are Kaplan and Francis turbines.
Brieﬂy, Kaplan’s disposition of shaft is in vertical direction whilst Francis can be either in horizontal or
vertical direction. In addition, Francis turbine has a larger number of blades that are not adjustable oppositely
to Kaplan turbines. Another basic difference is that Kaplan turbines are more suitable low head and large
ﬂow condition whereas Francis turbines are employed for medium head and ﬂow applications [46]. The
power produced by a turbine can be calculated using the following expression [62]:
Pt(t) = qt(t)kt∆ht(t)
(2.46)
==PAGE:58==
28
CHAPTER 2. MODELLING OF WATER SYSTEMS
Figure 2.16: Two main types of turbines (A- Reaction Turbine, B- Impulse Turbine) [73].
where kt is the turbine coefﬁcient, [Jm−4], and ∆ht(t) is the turbine head, [m]. Similarly to the deﬁnition
of the pump head, if ∆hT is the height difference of the duct of the turbine and it is assumed that the turbine
is fed with water from a surface with a water level equal to hlv1(t) and the used water ends in a surface with
a water level of hlv2(t), then:
∆ht(t) = ∆hT + hlv1(t) −hlv2(t)
(2.47)
The biggest hydroelectric power plant in Europe is located at La Muela, Spain, with a generated power of
852 MWh.
2.7
Conclusion
The aim of this chapter was to present the most popular methods regarding the modelling of water systems.
After the development of Saint Venant equations with the aid of mass and momentum conservation laws,
various ways to transform the PDEs to ODEs were described whereas the MoL was chosen as the most
suitable one. Afterwards, as system identiﬁcation methods can not be applied in this thesis due to the lack of
real data, ID model was introduced as an alternative that may lead to reduction of the computational load
that the Saint Venant equations produce. As a matter of fact, ID model divides the reach into two parts; the
part of the uniform ﬂow and the part of backwater area. It was also explained that the two key parameters
of the ID model are the time delay and the backwater area as well as how they are affected by different
ﬂow conditions in the river. This makes inevitable the need to tune ID model according to the results of the
simulation of Saint Venant equations. Finally, a short discussion regarding modelling of various hydraulic
structures that often interact with the river system took place.
==PAGE:59==
CHAPTER 3
Control Techniques
3.1
Intoduction
Thus far, the modelling of river systems has been treated theoretically where, of course, the aim of this
procedure was to obtain a solid background in order to be able to later establish a valid model for control
purposes. At this point, a choice for the ﬁnal control strategy that will be implemented should be made. This
chapter gives an overview of the most frequently used control strategies for river systems while the two most
popular control techniques, Linear Quadratic Regulator (LQR) and Model Predictive Control (MPC), are
developed in detail. The greatest similarity of these two control techniques is that both try to minimize a
performance index (also called cost function). This means that the most suitable control action is the one that
leads to the minimum or maximum (it depends on the formulation of the problem) value of the index. Hence,
these strategies belong to the class of optimal controllers. Additionally, special care should be given in order
to avoid infeasibility regarding MPC and this chapter will introduce the most frequently used method to face
this threat; slack variables. Moreover, the advantages of MPC will be highlighted whereas the way that it
can be practically implemented in a typical river system will also be described. Ultimately, the concept of
Economic Model Predictive Control (EMPC) is introduced together with the mathematical formulation of
the problem and a short discussion about the stability of EMPC.
3.2
Feedback and Feedforward Control
As it may be expected, the ﬁrst controllers that were used in order to control water systems were the
traditional feedback and feedforward controllers. The basic goal is to keep the water levels close to the
desired values. As it is widely known, the core idea behind feedback control (or closed loop control) is ﬁrstly
the measurement of water levels in each sampling instant and then the comparison with the set point values.
Afterwards, depending on the results of the comparison, the controller produces the appropriate control
signal. Typical representative of this type of controllers is the Proportional Integral controller (PI controller).
In the vast majority of the cases, extensive tuning is needed in order to determine the proportional and the
integral gains. In fact, there are numerous successful applications of feedback control concerning river
systems, [5] , [40], [57], [64], where the designed feedback control system is robustly stable and adequately
fast.
Furthermore, in feedforward control (or open loop control) the knowledge (either measurement or prediction)
of the disturbances is exploited using the inverse model of the disturbances’ effect on the system. Howbeit,
model uncertainties and inaccurate measurements often lead to deviation from the desired set points. One
frequent solution to this problem is the combination of a feedforward controller with a feedback controller in
29
==PAGE:60==
30
CHAPTER 3. CONTROL TECHNIQUES
order to correct the errors introduced by the act of the feedforward action. The typical control scheme for
the combination of feedback and feedforward control is shown in Figure 3.1. The main disadvantage of this
strategy is that the resulting controller is not aware of the existing constraints (ﬂood levels of the river for
example). Fruitful implementations of this type of control tactic concerning water systems can be found in
[45] and[77].
Figure 3.1: Combination of feedback and feedforward control for a water system [76].
3.3
Linear Quadratic Regulator
3.3.1
Introduction
LQR belongs to the family of optimal controllers whilst the term optimal implies that the control action is
determined based on the minimization of a cost function. Successful implementations of LQR for water
systems can be found in [25], [56], [79]. The development of LQR technique takes place below and is
mainly inspired by [30].
3.3.2
Formulation of Quadratic Cost Function
Starting from the general case and given a linearised, time varying plant and expressing the states and
the inputs as the incremental ones (the term incremental represents the deviation from the stationary
values), the state space formulation is:
˙x(t) = A(t)x(t) + B(t)u(t)
y(t) = C(t)x(t) + D(t)u(t)
(3.1)
where A(t) ∈Rnx×nx, B(t) ∈Rnx×nu, C(t) ∈Rny×nx, D(t) ∈Rny×nu and nx is the number of states,
nu the number of inputs and ny the number of measurements. If an input step is termed r(t) and the output
response is y(t), the most common choice is a quadratic performance index of the form:
J =
Z ∞
0
(r(t) −y(t))2 ∂t
(3.2)
==PAGE:61==
3.3. LINEAR QUADRATIC REGULATOR
31
Usually, the output vector y(t) is replaced by the state vector x(t) and consequently the cost function
becomes closely related to the energy of the system. Moreover, it is evident that the cost function should
also include a term related to the control signal which has to be limited in size and bandwidth. Supposing
that t0 is the starting point and that it is desired to optimize the control over some interval up to a ﬁnite time
t1, the result is a cost function with the following form:
J =
Z t1
t0
[xT (t)Q(t)x(t) + uT (t)R(t)u(t)]∂t
(3.3)
Often, a ﬁnal time state cost term is added as a quadratic term and consequently the resulting quadratic
performance index is:
J = 1
2xT (t1)S(t1)x(t1) + 1
2
Z t1
t0
[xT (t)Q(t)x(t) + uT (t)R(t)u(t)]∂t
(3.4)
The matrices S(t1) ∈Rnx×nx, Q(t) ∈Rnx×nx and R(t) ∈Rnu×nu are the weight matrices which
essentially represent the amount of impact of the deviations of x(t1), x(t) and u(t) on the overall cost
function. As the cost function should be bounded from below in order to guarantee the existence of a
minimum, the terms in the index should be non-negative whereas u(t) must be bounded for all t. Hence, the
following conditions should be satisﬁed:
S(t1) ≥0
Q(t) ≥0
∀t ∈[t0, t1]
R(t) > 0
∀t ∈[t0, t1]
(3.5)
To understand better the role of these weight matrices, it would be prudent to consider the case where the
element [Q]m,n, with m, n = 1, · · · , nx, is large. The result of this will be a heavy penalty for the product
of states xm and xn in the cost function and subsequently the control law will try to minimize the deviation
of this state as much as possible. Furthermore, it should be pointed out that the overall behaviour of the
control system is highly inﬂuenced by the ratio between the elements of Q(t) and R(t). If, for example,
the elements of Q(t) are chosen to be large compared to these of R(t) then the system states will tend to
respond faster but the price would be an increased control signal. Oppositely, the response would be slow
and the deviation of the states larger but the inputs are kept as small as possible. Besides, the weight matrix
S(t1) penalizes errors in the ﬁnal state. Taking into consideration all the above, it is obvious that in order to
reach an acceptable performance of the control system, extensive tuning is an inevitable requirement. A
general rule for the selection of the weight matrices is:
[S(t)]i,i =
1
max[xi(t1)]2
[Q(t)]i,i =
1
(t1 −t0)max[xi(t)]2
[R(t)]j,j =
1
(t1 −t0)max[uj(t)]2
(3.6)
where i = 1, 2, · · · , nx and j = 1, 2, · · · , nu. This rule of course constitutes just a starting point. Note that
cross terms may be used if interaction between some states or inputs is needed.

3.3.3
Discrete Time Optimal Control
As a matter of fact, nowadays, the majority of systems is controlled digitally and this will be the case for the

starting point is the Discrete Linear Time Invariant (DLTI) state space model:
x(k + 1) = Adx(k) + Bdu(k)
y(k) = Cydx(k) + Ddu(k)
(3.7)

approximated by the following expression:

3.3.4
Steady State Discrete Optimal Control
The LQR controller presented above has a time varying gain matrix, Kd(k). This means that even for time
invariant systems, the control of the plant over Np samples, requires the off-line calculation and storage
of Np × nu signals. Thereby, it would be desirable if a sub-optimal, but constant, LQR gain was the case.
Actually, if the solution of Riccati equation converges to a constant value denoted as P d∞, this will be a
solution to the steady state discrete time algebraic Riccati equation which may be expressed as:
P d∞= Qd + AT
d [P d∞−P d∞Bd(Rd + BT
d P d∞Bd)−1BT
d P d∞]Ad
(3.19)
The previous equation was established using the following equality, P d(k) = P d(k + 1) = P d∞. Equation
3.19 represents a set of at most n2

semi-deﬁnite or negative deﬁnite or even complex solutions. So, Riccati equation may not have any positive
semi-seﬁnite solutions. However, if there is such a solution, the steady state feedback controller is given by:
u(k) = −Kd∞x(k) = −[Rd + BT
d P d∞Bd]−1BT
d P d∞Adx(k)
(3.20)
Conclusively, a feedback optimal discrete time control with constant gain matrix has been established.
Nevertheless, the main drawback of standard LQR is that it cannot handle the existing constraints of the

3.3.5
Linear Quadratic Regulator for Reference Tracking
The control action of Equation 3.20 always tries to lead the system to the origin. Howbeit, in many cases it
is a requirement that the output of the system follows a certain reference signal, r(k). Note that only step
changes of the reference signal are taken into consideration here. Assume the discrete, linear, time invariant
state space model:
x(k + 1) = Adx(k) + Bdu(k) + Edd(k)
y(k) = Cydx(k) + Ddu(k)
(3.21)
where Ed ∈Rnx×nd and nd is the number of disturbance inputs. In order to achieve tracking of a step input
r(k), the feedback law should obtain the following form [27]:
u(k) = uv(k) −Kd(k) (x(k) −xv(k))
(3.22)
where xv ∈Rnx×1 and uv ∈Rnu×1 are calculated using the following relation which exploits the system’s

As explained before regarding the constraints for the control actions, the control law does not know the
constraints regarding the outputs, either. The only way to obtain a behaviour that will respect the existing
constraints is excessive tuning of the LQR controller but without any guarantee for satisfactory results and
certain avoidance of the constraints violation. Apart from that, it should be pointed out that in order this
tactic to result to an accurate tracking of a reference, no mismatch should exist between the model and the
real plant whereas a perfect knowledge about the future disturbances should exist.
3.4
Model Predictive Control
3.4.1
Introduction
Model Predictive Control (MPC) or Receding Horizon Control (RHC) or Moving Horizon Control (MHC),
was ﬁrstly introduced back in the late seventies and nowadays is considered an advanced control strategy. To
be more speciﬁc, the name MPC does not refer explicitly to one type of controller but rather to a group of
control methods which share some common features:
• Use of the model of the plant in order to predict the outputs at future time instants (horizon).
• The control sequence is calculated with respect to the minimization of an objective function.
==PAGE:65==
3.4. MODEL PREDICTIVE CONTROL
35
• Only the ﬁrst control signal is applied in the system at each time step while afterwards the horizon is
moved towards the future having obtained new measurements and observations (receding horizon).
As a matter of fact, following this policy feedback action is introduced.
The most attractive feature of MPC is the ability to naturally incorporate constraints into the optimization
problem. This is the main reason behind the choice of MPC for a really wide range of sectors starting from
petrochemical industry to food processing and aerospace applications. The implementation of MPC has
become more frequent as nowadays computer power is cheaper and easily accessible. There is a vast amount
of successful applications of MPC regarding the control of water systems. Some of them can be found in [4],
[10], [21], [26], [76]. The development of MPC strategy follows below and is mainly based on [14], [43],
[55], [80].
3.4.2
Basic Concept
Assume again the linearized, discrete time, state-space model of a plant:
x(k + 1) = Adx(k) + Bdu(k)
y(k) = Cydx(k)
z(k) = Czdx(k)
(3.24)
where Ad ∈Rnx×nx, Bd ∈Rnx×nu, Cyd ∈Rny×nx, Czd ∈Rnz×nx, ny is the number of measurements
and nz is the number of controlled variables. Of course, very often vectors y and z overlap to a great extent
while sometimes they are even identical. The basic concept of MPC is depicted in Figure 3.2 and may be
summarized in the following points:
• The future values of the controlled variables z(k+i|k)2, i = 1, · · · , Np, where Np is called prediction
horizon and k represents the current sampling instant, and the future control signals, u(k + i|k), i =
0, · · · , Nu −1 are predicted based on the past values of inputs and the current values of the outputs.
Nu is called the control horizon and it should always hold true that Nu ≤Np. For this project it is
always considered that Nu = Np. In addition, it is assumed that the prediction horizon is long enough
in order to ensure stability. More about the stability of MPC can be found at Appendix A.4.
• MPC produces a sequence of control inputs in order to keep the process as close as possible to a
reference trajectory r(k + i), by taking into consideration the predicted behaviour of the plant over a
ﬁnite prediction horizon Np and solving a cost function of the form:
argmin
u(k),u(k+1),··· ,u(k+Np−1)
J(x, u, k, Np)
(3.25)
subject to the set of equations in 3.24. In most cases, the criterion J(x, u, k, Np) is quadratic and as
result the optimisation problem can be expressed as a typical quadratic problem which includes the
control effort while the existing constraints of the system are often expressed as linear inequalities.
As already stated the most appealing feature of MPC is the ability to handle the existing constraints.
When this cost function has one optimal solution which is globally optimal ∀i ∈[0, Np −1] then the
2Note that this notation is used also in Figure 3.2 and means that the value of the variable at the time instant k + i is calculated at time k
==PAGE:66==
36
CHAPTER 3. CONTROL TECHNIQUES
problem is said to be convex. More about convex quadratic problems can be found at Appendix A.3.
Note that this thesis will treat only convex optimization problems.
• It should be stated that only the ﬁrst control input sample u(k|k) is applied to the plant each time. At
time k + 1 new measurements are available and the control input u(k + 1|k + 1), which is in most
of the cases different than u(k + 1|k), is calculated using the receding horizon principle. The whole
process is repeated until the end of the simulation.
Figure 3.2: Basic principle of MPC [14].
3.4.3
Alternative State Variable Choices
In order to avoid steady state errors, it is advisable to modify the conventional state space formulation,
which was described previously, in order the predictive algorithm to produce the rate of change of the inputs,
∆u(k) = u(k) −u(k −1), rather than inputs, u(k) [43]. Thus, in the cost function to be minimized by
MPC, the input rates of change are penalized rather than the inputs themselves. This comes as a consequence
of the control input needing some nonzero value at steady state. This would make the cost function to
increase since the optimal solution is that the output has some deviation from the reference while at the same
time less control action is used when the conventional state space formulation is employed. On the other
hand, when the alternative state -space formulation is used, the rate of change is zero at steady state so the
cost function is minimized. Thereby, it makes sense to consider the discrete time integration from ∆u to u
as part of the plant dynamics. In addition, it is convenient to regard that the controller is producing the signal
∆u and the plant having it as input. The conﬁguration of the system with the MPC following this design
procedure can be seen in Figure 3.3. If the state vector is redeﬁned as:

3.4.6
Soft Constrained MPC
One of the most threatening problems that can emerge with the predictive control is that the optimizer
may face an infeasible problem. This is a very dangerous situation since when infeasibility is reached, the
optimizer does not return a solution and thus there is no control authority to the system which actually
becomes open loop. In fact, there are lots of cases that can lead to a situation like this. For example, a large
disturbance may mean that there is no way that the system can be retained within the existing constraints
with limited control authority. Apart from that, if there is a difference between the real system and the
==PAGE:74==
44
CHAPTER 3. CONTROL TECHNIQUES
internal model used by the controller, the optimizer may think that large disturbances are the cause behind
this discrepancy and eventually decide that it does not have the appropriate control authority to keep the
plant within the constraints. Additionally, infeasibility is sometimes the result of wrong MPC set-up like
choice of short prediction horizon. Thereby, many methods have been proposed to deal with this serious
problem like constraint management where the least important constraint is "softened" in order to try to
regain feasibility or producing the same control signal as the previous step, to name but a few [43].
Probably the most systematic way to face the threat of infeasibility is to "soften" the constraints. In other
words, to allow the optimizer to violate the constraints which are no longer regarded as "hard" boundaries.
However, soft constraints should be introduced together with a high penalty in order to ensure that optimizer
will violate the constraints only when it is absolutely needed in order to avoid infeasibility. Furthermore, it
should be underlined that in most cases output constraints are "softened" whilst input constraints are kept
"hard". This fact absolutely makes sense as for example valves, pumps and in general actuators have a
limited range of action which cannot be exceeded.
The standard way to implement soft constraints is the introduction of slack variables, n. Thereby, the
"softened" output constraints can be expressed as:
zi
≤
zmax + ni,max
zi
≥
zmin −ni,min
ni,max
≥
0
ni,min
≥
0
It is apparent that when the slack variables are equal to zero, they are inactive whereas when they are
positive, they become active. Setting nmin = (
