It should be mentioned that despite the fact that slack variables allow the implementation of soft constraints
and as a result the avoidance of infeasibility, they also lead to large computational times as the optimization
is conducted with respect to more variables. It is well known that heavy computational burden is always a
problem that should be seriously considered when it comes to MPC. In Chapter 6, various methods dealing
with this challenge will be applied and evaluated.
3.5
Economic Model Predictive Control (EMPC)
3.5.1
Basic Concept of EMPC
In this section, Economic Model Predictive Control (EMPC), a method which has started arising in the
recent years, is introduced. In industrial applications, a common practice is to use Real Time Optimization
(RTO) software that calculates the optimal operating points for the controlled variables of a system, with
respect to the system’s economics, and afterwards a MPC is set to regulate the system around these operating
points using a cost function that penalizes deviations from them. On the other hand, Economic MPC is
able to incorporate the economics of the system directly in its cost function. So, rather than computing the
appropriate control signals for the plant in order to track the desired setpoints, it is computing the control
signals that make the plant operation more proﬁtable. Conclusively, EMPC provides a MPC framework that
minimizes the cost of operations subject to the existing constraints of the system.
3.5.2
Mathematical Problem Formulation
In the general case, the optimization problem can be solved using linear programming (LP). The cost function
can be formulated as:
min
∆U φ =
Np−1
X
i=0
(cT
Y,i+1yi+1 + cT
U,iui)
(3.88)
cY,i+1 represents the proﬁt the outputs generate from the operation of the system, while cU,i represents the
cost of using the control inputs. These costs can represent money or energy and can be time varying, whereas
in many cases cY is omitted. The cost function in Equation 3.88 is subject to state and input constraints as
deﬁned in Equations 3.65 and 3.68.
The problem arising for this cost function is that it results in an optimum that is an extreme point and small
changes in the operation of the system can cause change of the optimal solution [32]. In Figure 3.4 this
problem is exhibited for linear program with two inputs and two outputs. Green color depicts the constraints
of inputs while red shows the constraints of outputs. The arrows demonstrate possible optimal solutions
which are located at one of the vertexes. One suggested solution is to narrow a bit the region of feasible
solutions and thus be more robust.
==PAGE:77==
3.5. ECONOMIC MODEL PREDICTIVE CONTROL (EMPC)
47
Figure 3.4: Example of a LP problem [32].
However, the remedy for this problem here is to include terms that penalize excessive rate of the control
inputs. This however, results in a less proﬁtable operation. As a matter of fact, there is a tradeoff between
optimality and robustness since the most proﬁtable operation of the plant may cause nonsmooth solutions for
the control inputs which increase wear and tear of the actuators of the system. For this reason, the absolute
value of the rate of change of the control inputs is also penalized and the cost function becomes:
min
∆U φ =
Np−1
X
i=0
(cT
Y,i+1yi+1 + cT
U,iui + RT
eco|∆ui|)
(3.89)
where Reco ∈Rnu×1 and its j-th element is penalizing the rate of change of the j-th control input
(j = 1, ..., nu). Including soft constraints for the controlled outputs, in order to ensure solution feasibility in
the case of extreme conditions, the cost function takes the ﬁnal form:
min
∆U,n φ =
Np−1
X
i=0
(cT
Y,i+1yi+1 + cT
U,iui + RT
eco|∆ui| + ST
neconi+1)
(3.90)
subject to:
Zc,min −nmin ≤Zc ≤Zc,max + nmax
(3.91)
with Sneco ∈Rnz×1. Moreover, since Sneco penalizes the use of the slack variables its elements should be
selected large enough in order to avoid the violation of the constraints whenever possible. One really crucial
detail that has to be emphasized is that the prediction horizon Np should be long enough in order to beneﬁt
as much as possible from possibly low input costs at some time instants keeping of course in mind that a
longer prediction horizon Np results to a heavier computational load.
3.5.3
Stability
Undoubtedly, stability of EMPC is a topic that should be considered carefully and there are many suggestions
in the literature summarized by [23]. As a matter of fact, as discussed in Appendix A.4 for nominal MPC, the
ﬁrst course of action that guarantees stability is to set prediction horizon, Np, to inﬁnity. Howbeit, although
many schemes have been proposed in order to solve such a problem, it remains generally difﬁcult to solve an
==PAGE:78==
48
CHAPTER 3. CONTROL TECHNIQUES
optimization problem with an inﬁnite number of decision variables. Alternatively, terminal constraints can
be employed to ensure stability of EMPC. To be more clear, at each sampling time, the optimization problem
is solved having the optimal equilibrium point of the inﬁnite horizon optimization as a terminal constraint.
The drawback of this method is the fact that it requires more computational time whilst the region of feasible
solutions is shrunk.
Prof of the EMPC stability includes the formulation of suitable Lyapunov functions. The core difference with
standard MPC is that with EMPC the optimal cost cannot be employed. Approaches for the determination of
appropriate Lyapunov functions can be found in [1] and [22]. Howbeit, at this thesis the topic of the stability
of EMPC will no longer be treated. Thus, it will be assumed that prediction and control horizon Np and Nu
(remember that for this thesis always Np = Nu) are long enough compared to the system’s dynamics in
order to guarantee stability [31].
3.6
Practical Implementation of MPC
A very critical component of MPC is its internal model which represents the dynamics of the plant and
is used in order to predict the water levels and ﬂows over the prediction horizon. As it has been already
signalized, this internal model should be simple enough in order to avoid large computational times but
without disregarding essential dynamics of the system. Except from this, the cost function and the constraints
are the most important factors that determine the behaviour of the controlled system. It has to be noted that a
state estimator, for example a Kalman ﬁlter, is of vital importance for the calculation of the control law, as
MPC is designed based on all the states of the system.
Apart from that, it is clear that the equalities that describe the water systems present severe non-linearities.
One main reason behind this non-linearity is usually the used structures, such as gates, as it can be clearly
seen in Equations 2.39, 2.40 and 2.41. Thereby, this non-linearity can be eliminated if MPC produces
the desired ﬂows and then a local ﬂow controller transform this ﬂow to the appropriate gate height. This
ﬂow controller can be a PID controller that uses the measurements of the water levels as well as the
inverted structure formula. This conﬁguration is depicted in Figure 3.5 and this type of control is known as
Master-Slave control [64].
3.7
Other Control Techniques
As described in [7], another possible way to control water systems is the use of heuristic methods where the
system is considered to be a black box and physical laws are not taken into consideration. Typical examples
of this method are neural networks control, fuzzy logic control and genetic algorithm control. As there are
no mathematical tools available to evaluate the performance of these methods, they should be calibrated
based on the real system or a simulation model. It should be pointed out that these methods are referred
only for reasons of completeness since only in extremely rare occasions they are preferred when it comes to
control of water systems.
Besides, in many river systems around the world the three-position controller is used as it is relies on
some very simple rules in order to achieve the desired regulation of water levels [7]. More speciﬁcally, the
controller does not react when the water levels are between some deﬁned limits and react appropriately when
these limits are violated. For example, when the water levels are low the gate is raised in order also the water
==PAGE:79==
3.8. EVALUATION OF MPC
49
Figure 3.5: Combination of MPC with a local PID controller [61].
level to increase. Obviously, these if-then-else rules lead to control actions based on local information and
do not take into account known disturbances. Consequently, they can hardly be used efﬁciently in order to
avoid ﬂoods. As it can be easily understood, the good performance of such a controller depends heavily on
the experience of the authority that formulates the control rules.
3.8
Evaluation of MPC
After the presentation of all the available control strategies, it becomes overt that MPC has some striking
advantages when compared to the other control methods. Especially for water systems, MPC presents the
following appealing features:
• Feedforward control is naturally introduced in order to take into account measurable or predicted
disturbances like expected rainfalls and water orders from consumers.
• MPC can easily treat MIMO (Multiple Input Multiple Output) systems oppositely to traditional control
tactics. As a matter of fact, water systems are typical MIMO systems.
• Existing constraints for the control signals and the outputs can easily be incorporated during the design
of the controller. As far as water systems are concerned, it is highly possible that constraints regarding
the water levels for ﬂood prevention, the capacity of the pumps and the rate of change of the ﬂow at
the gates exist and of course, should be borne in mind during the solution of the optimization problem.
Apparently, taking into consideration all the constraints, the effectiveness and the safety of the control
operation are noticeably enhanced.
• The optimization problem demonstrates a great amount of generality and thereby can be easily
customized according to the needs of the speciﬁc problem. The only requirement is that the problem
can be formulated as an optimization problem that can be solved fast enough with respect to the
control sampling time.
• MPC can be used both for ﬂood control and set point control.
==PAGE:80==
50
CHAPTER 3. CONTROL TECHNIQUES
Besides these strong points, MPC presents also some drawbacks as is logical. First and foremost, the fact
that MPC solves an optimization problem at each time step results to heavy computational load especially if
there are numerous constraints and states. Furthermore, as the controller has to return the control signal that
should be implemented, the optimization needs to be solved faster than the sampling time of the controller.
Fortunately, the rivers are characterised by relatively slow dynamics. In addition, MPC derivation is more
complex than this of traditional controllers like PID. This is the main reason behind the choice of traditional
controllers for small and fast systems. It is certain also that MPC needs tuning in order to ﬁnd the weight
matrices which result to the desired behaviour. Finally, in order to fully exploit the advantages of MPC,
an accurate model of the plant is needed. In Chapter 5, MPC will be compared with LQR in simple water
systems using many simulation scenarios so that the aforementioned advantages become even more clear.
3.9
Conclusion
The chapter demonstrated the available control methods for the control of typical water systems. It was
explained that traditional control methods like feedback and feedforward controllers are maybe suitable
for set-point control but totally inappropriate for ﬂood prevention. Apart from that, standard LQR, which
belongs to the class of optimal controllers, has been used extensively for control of river systems but
without being able to handle the existing constraints. Moreover, MPC was developed in detail whilst soft
constraints were introduced with the help of slack variables in order to avoid infeasibility. Apart from that, a
theoretical introduction to EMPC was given in order its differences with nominal MPC to become crystal
clear. Finally, it was explained how MPC can be combined with local PID controllers when it comes to
practical implementation in water systems. The highlight of the chapter was the evaluation of MPC that
revealed the superiority of MPC over the other presented control methods. However, it should always be
kept in mind that the optimization problem should be solved fast enough in order to achieve robust control
of the water systems.
==PAGE:81==
CHAPTER 4
Model Validation
4.1
Intoduction
In Chapter 2 the various existing options about modelling of water systems were presented. It was seen
that these methods span from Saint-Venant equations, which give a very accurate but computational heavy
model, to ID model which leads to a simple and compact model. The ﬁrst goal of this chapter will be to
linearize and discretize in time and space the Saint Venant equations and obtain a state space model for
which controllers can easily be designed and tested. Afterwards, ID model will be treated and it is clear
that some important constants should be identiﬁed in order to establish a valid model. In addition, after the
formulation of the ID model, it should be evaluated by comparing it with the non-linear model as well as
with this of the discretized (in space and time) Saint Venant equations. The main goal of the chapter is to
verify whether ID model captures all the essential dynamics of the water system needed for control reasons
and consequently if the discretized Saint Venant equations can be disregarded concerning control design.
4.2
Application of MoL for a Single Reach Model
Unfortunately, the non-linear character of Saint Venant equations, which model the water levels and
discharges of a water system, does not allow to reach a close-form solution. Nevertheless, using MoL, which
was presented back in Section 2.2.4, the PDEs can be transformed into a set of ODEs. The assumptions
under which such a conversion is meaningful, are:
• Each reach of the river system is divided into N cells of equal length, ∆x.
• qi(t) is the value of the discharge in the middle of the cell i, hi(t) is the value of the water level at the
beginning of cell i and hN+1(t) represents the water level at the end of the reach.
• qin(t) and qout(t) represent the water inﬂow at the beginning of the reach and the water outﬂow at the
end of the reach, respectively. Additionally, qli is the total lateral ﬂow of cell i.
• The width along each cell is assumed to remain constant. So, if w1 and wN+1 are the width at the
beginning and at the end of the reach, respectively, and supposing that the width along the reach
changes linearly then the river width at the beginning of each cell i, can be calculated from the
following relation:
wi = w1 + (i −1)(wN+1 −w1)
N
,
i = 2, · · · , N
(4.1)
• The river bed slope, I0, is assumed to remain constant along the reach.

The reach proﬁle is depicted in Figure 4.2. The results are in ﬁne accordance with the theoretical ﬁnding
since it is obvious that the reach has a part where the ﬂow is uniform (water levels h1 to h4) and one part
that is affected by backwater (water levels h5 to h9). This results to a water level, with respect to the river
bed, that is almost constant along the part of the uniform ﬂow whereas it is increased gradually along the
backwater area.
Figure 4.2: River reach proﬁle.
==PAGE:87==
4.3. LINEARIZATION AND DISCRETIZATION OF SAINT VENANT EQUATIONS
57
Using now the set of Equations 4.11 and linearizing around the found stationary point, the state space model
that consists of the matrices Asv, Bsv, Csv and Dsv can be obtained exploiting the matrices of Equations
4.12 and 4.13. The poles of the transfer function from the upstream ﬂow, qin, to the water level at the end of
the reach, h9, is presented in Figure 4.3 where it can be seen that the poles of the system are very close to
the imaginary axis. This conﬁrms the argumentation that river systems have relatively slow dynamics. Note
that there is a group of ﬁve poles very close to zero that are not clearly visible in the pole map. Of course,
the transfer function from downstream input to the water level at the end of the reach has the same poles.
Finally, the zeros of both transfer functions were found to be located at the left half plane.
Figure 4.3: Pole map for the continuous system from upstream or downstream input to the water level at the
end of the reach.
Making an impulse and a unit step test from input qin, which is located upstream, at t = 2.77 hours, using
the linearised Saint Venant equations, the results dramatized in Figures 4.4 and 4.5 are obtained, respectively.
The outﬂow at the end of the reach stays constantly at 20 m3/s and the depicted water levels represent
deviations from the stationary values. An important ﬁnding that can be elicited with the aid of the impulse
test is that while the water levels at the cells located at the part of uniform ﬂow present an increase which
fades away quickly, the water levels located at the backwater area present a constant deviation from the
stationary point existing before the impulse test. This shows clearly that the extra water entered the system is
stored at the backwater area of the reach. Furthermore, regarding the uniform ﬂow part, the closer a point is
to the beginning of the reach the more sharply is affected by the impulse change.
Similarly, for the unit step test, the water levels of the uniform ﬂow part reach a new stationary point and
remain there (note that again moving from point 1 to 4 the behaviour is more sluggish) whereas the water
levels of the backwater area increase constantly as long as the unit step in qin is applied (from t = 2.77hours
and onwards). In addition, it can be be concluded that along the uniform ﬂow part, the water levels react one
after another whereas at the backwater area the water levels are increased all together almost simultaneously.
As a matter of fact, this means that the time delay connected with the travelling of water from an upstream
point to a downstream, is connected mainly with the uniform ﬂow part.
The next step is the discretization in time of the linearised Saint Venant equations obtained previously (for
more information see Appendix A.1). Examining the time constants of the system it is found that the smallest
==PAGE:88==
58
CHAPTER 4. MODEL VALIDATION
Figure 4.4: Water levels at the end of all cells for an impulse test from qin.
Figure 4.5: Water levels at the end of all cells for a unit step test from qin.
time constant, is 12s. According to Shannon theorem [30], the sampling time should be equal to or smaller
than the half of this time constant. Nevertheless, the dynamics at the intermediate points of the discretization
are not of interest, since the control input is desired to regulate the water level at the end of the reach only, so
the sampling time can be chosen with respect to the dominating time delay as suggested in [30]. In other
words, it is not needed to have a sampling time with which the effect of the control input would not be seen
at the output for several samples. In the subsequent section, it will be shown that for this reach, the time
delay can vary from 1500 s to 2700 s, depending on the operating point.
Moreover, in [2], it is advised to select the sampling time at least four times smaller than the rise time 1 of
the system. In this case, the water level at the end of the reach does not reach a steady state since more water
is accumulated in a step input on the upstream ﬂow while keeping the downstream ﬂow constant. Thus, the
rise time of the ﬂow at the last cell is examined, which is what affects the ﬁnal water level. The rise time
was found to be around 3800 −4100 s, depending on the operating point (a rough estimation can also be
made using Figure 4.7). Therefore, taking into consideration both methods addressed, the sampling time
was chosen to be Ts = 1000 s. Unquestionably, faster sampling time could mean better performance, up to a
point, but on the other hand, faster sampling frequency would mean more states for the ID model, something
1Rise time is the time needed in order to reach 63% of the steady state.
==PAGE:89==
4.3. LINEARIZATION AND DISCRETIZATION OF SAINT VENANT EQUATIONS
59
that is undesired due to the increase of the computation burden. It is seen that the choice of the sampling time
is a trade-off which varies with the desired properties of each design. It has to be pointed out that it has been
veriﬁed, for all the reaches that will be used later, that this sampling time agrees with the concept that was
explained here and that the response of the discretized model matches the response of the linearized model
at the sampling instants. The pole map of the discrete system with this sampling time from the upstream or
downstream input to the water level at the end of the reach, is depicted in Figure 4.6.
Figure 4.6: Pole map for the discrete system from upstream or downstream input to the water level at the
end of the reach.
Carrying now a step test where qin steps from 20 m3/s to 30 m3/s and qout remains constant at 20 m3/s ,
the results depicted in Figures 4.7, 4.8, 4.9 and 4.10 are obtained. Note that the title "Linearized S.Venant" at
the legends, concerns the continuous time linearized Saint Venant equations, while "Discretized S. Venant"
concerns the linearized and discretized in time Saint Venant equations. First and foremost, it is indicative
that both the linearized continuous and the discretized system are very close to the full non-linear system
since they present small differences mainly away from the linearization point. In addition, one interesting
deduction is that the ﬂow decreases moving from the upstream level to the downstream after the step change
as a result of the existing boundary conditions. Now, it can be understood better why in Figure 4.5 and 4.8
the parts of the uniform ﬂow jump to a new stationary point. More speciﬁcally, the points located at the
uniform ﬂow jump successively to the new input ﬂow (30 m3/s) and therefore this leads to (see Equations
4.6, 4.7 and 4.8) an increase to the water levels that lay between these ﬂows. For the points located at the
backwater area, it is seen that the ﬂows obtain constant values larger than 20 m3/s (boundary condition at
the end of the reach) and smaller than 30 m3/s (boundary condition at the beginning of the reach) whilst
the closer is the point to the end of the reach the lower value has the ﬂow there at steady state after the step
change. Therefore, the water levels at the backwater area increase constantly (see Figures 4.9 and 4.10).
Conclusively, it can be stated that the discretized in time and space Saint Venant equations provide a
trustworthy model that follows closely the Saint Venant equations regarding the single reach. The striking
problem here is the high number of states; 17 for this speciﬁc reach that consists of 8 cells. Therefore,
a simpliﬁed model should be found and validated and the strongest candidate is the ID model that was
presented theoretically back in Chapter 2.

4.4
Simpliﬁed Reach Model Using ID Model
The followed strategy here is the division of the river into large sections in order to end up with one section
between each pair of hydraulic structures (gates for example). As it has been already explained in Section
2.3, the resulting model for each reach consists of a ﬁrst order model including a time delay:
h(k + 1) −h(k) = Ts
As
(qin(k −kd) + qout(k))
(4.16)
where lateral off-takes and intakes are disregarded. Before investigating the essential concern around the ID
model which is whether it is able to capture all the essential dynamics of a river reach, the backwater area,
As, and the delay time steps, kd, should be identiﬁed. The ﬁrst way to do so is by exploiting the geometrical
features of the canal. Howbeit, this procedure needs real data from the canal and therefore it is difﬁcult to be
applied here. The second one suggests an unsteady ﬂow simulation in order to determine these important
parameters [66]. In Figure 4.11 the impact of three step changes in qin on the water level at the end of
the reach is depicted; from 5 m3/s to 10 m3/s (low ﬂow pattern), from 20 m3/s to 30 m3/s (medium
ﬂow pattern) and from 60 m3/s to 80 m3/s (high ﬂow pattern). Note that deviations from the stationary
values are shown while the ﬁrst vertical dotted line signalizes where the step change starts to apply. The
second vertical dotted line is used in order to represent the selected time delay. It is apparent though that
different ﬂow conditions result to different time of reaction for the water level at the end of the reach. More
speciﬁcally, Table 4.2 includes the identiﬁed time delay for these three tests as well as the calculated value
of the backwater area. A reliable value for the backwater area was obtained by applying the mass balance
law [76]:
As = ∆q∆t
∆h
(4.17)
As it can be seen in Figure 4.11, the tangent of the water level rise line for each step response has been used.
It is indicative that higher ﬂow conditions result to smaller time delay and smaller backwater area. This
totally agrees with the comments of Subsection 2.3.1 taking into consideration that it has been assumed that
the cross section of the river is rectangular. That means that as the ﬂow gets higher and the length of the
backwater area decreases, the width of the increased water level remains the same and thereby the backwater
area becomes smaller. The selected value for the time delay of this reach is chosen to be 2000s in order to
be close to the value of the low ﬂow condition since this constitutes the most challenging situation as it has
already been mentioned. Furthermore, the number of delay time steps should be an integer; here it is kd1 = 2
as the sampling time is Ts = 1000 s. On the other hand, backwater area is selected to be 740 ∗103 m2 after
excessive tests and tuning in order the ID model to give results close to these of the non-linear system.

loop test is conducted. As it can be seen in Figure 4.12 there are changes in qin and qout resulting to new
steady state conditions. It is clear from Figure 4.13 that ID model is very close to the non-linear as well
as to the discretized Saint Venant equations. Additionally, Figure 4.12 presents the difference of the water
level at the end of the reach when ID model and discretized Saint Venanant equations are used from the
respective water level when the non-linear model is employed and reveals that errors are present mainly for
steady states away from the linearization point while, in general, ID model presents higher relevant errors
compared to the discretized one. It should also be underlined that during low ﬂow conditions the errors are
generally larger compared to high ﬂow conditions. Ultimately, it goes without saying that the step change in
qin needs an amount of time equal to the time delay of the reach in order to start affecting the water level at
the end of the reach whereas a change in qout starts immediately to affect the water level since it is located at
the backwater area.
Apart from the previous tests, the Bode diagrams from upstream and downstream ﬂow to the water level at
the end of the reach for both methods are presented in Figure 4.14. Obviously, the two models are almost
identical for a frequency up to a decade below the Nyquist frequency 2 and therefore differences start to
emerge for frequencies larger than 0.0005 rad/sec. Only at this region of frequencies the discretized Saint
Venant equations present some resonance frequencies that cannot be captured by the ID model.
2Nyquist frequency is typically the half of the sampling rate of the system.

To conclude, from these open loop tests it can be deducted that ID model seems to give similar results as the
non-linear system and the discretized Saint Venant equations while it has signiﬁcantly less states; 3 states for
the speciﬁc reach, something that gives a reduction of 82% regarding the number of states. The next crucial
step is to evaluate the closed loop performance of these models.
4.5
Closed Loop Evaluation
At this point, a controller should be designed for the single reach under investigation. For this section, qin,
will be considered as a disturbance while qout will be the control variable and the controlled variable will be
the water level at the end of the reach, h9. Hence, an LQR controller based on the discretized Saint Venant
equations will be designed and another one using the ID model. Practically speaking, although Saint Venant
equations are mathematically fully controllable under this scheme, only the water levels of the backwater
==PAGE:95==

Figure 4.14: Bode diagrams from upstream (input 1) and downstream (input 2) ﬂow to the water level at the
end of the reach.
area can be controlled while there is no control authority at the part of uniform ﬂow which is only affected by
the disturbance qin. For the ID model, it is also indicative from Equations 4.18 and taking into consideration
that only qout is controlled, that only the dynamics of the backwater area are affected by the controller and
this results to a not fully controllable system. Moreover, the matrix Qd is always being selected so that
the pair (Ad,

Qd) is observable. This according to [30] guarantees an asymptotically stable closed loop
system for the LQR. Note that the procedure described in 3.3.5 was followed.
The selected weight matrices, in order to optimize the performance of the system with respect to its output,
are:

presents the disturbance change and the control action for both models as well as the ﬂows at the intermediate
discretization points of the reach. It should be mentioned that there is also a reference change regarding
the water level at the end of the reach as shown in Figure 4.18. Apparently, the control actions of the two
controllers are almost identical, and so are the ﬂows in the middle of the cells. It is evident also that the
ﬂows of the uniform ﬂow part are not affected by the control action oppositely to these of the backwater area.
Furthermore, it should be highlighted that during the reference change, the water levels located at the part of
the uniform ﬂow do not change whilst these of the backwater area are directly affected as revealed by Figures

level at the end of the reach and the reference value for both models. It is easy to see that both approaches

present similar errors which are higher especially during transient periods of reference or disturbance change

To sum up, the closed loop test showed that ID model has a similar performance when compared to the
discretized Saint Venant equations and thereby it can be decided that this simple model captures all the
essential dynamics of the river reach and thereafter it can be used later in order to design MPC and make an
initial but decisive step towards the reduction of computational burden.

4.6. CONCLUSION

4.6
Conclusion
Conclusively, this chapter dealt with the establishment of a model in order to simulate robustly an open
water reach while avoiding a high number of states. The ﬁrst step was to use the MoL in order to obtain
ODEs and then proceed with the linearization and the discretization in time of the Saint Venant equations.
Impulse and step changes validated the theoretical ﬁnding that the reach is divided in two parts; this of the
uniform ﬂow and this affected by the backwater area. In addition, the behaviour of the discretized system
was found to be very close to this of the non-linear system. In order to tackle the problem of the high
number of states, the ID model was introduced and the initial action was to identify two key parameters; the
time delay and the backwater area. Although different ﬂows lead to different values for these parameters
==PAGE:98==
68
CHAPTER 4. MODEL VALIDATION
Figure 4.19: Control action for the two designed controllers and disturbance signal qin, with the use of LQR.
and thereby make the need of tuning procedure inevitable, the selected values produced very promising
results. Particularly, step changes for the upstream and downstream input for the two models produced
similar results and Bode diagrams proved that for low frequencies the response of the two models is almost
identical. The last step was to design LQR controllers with the same weight matrices in order to evaluate the
closed loop behaviour. The results revealed that again the two models exhibit similar outcomes for reference
and disturbance changes. Taking all the above into consideration, it is clear that the ID model constitutes a
simple and reliable model that simulates accurately enough the dynamics of a single reach and can be used
later for control design. Last but not least, it should not be forgotten that the ID model is also more practical
since it needs the tuning of only two parameters, whilst the Saint Venant equations need the deﬁnition of
many more parameters.
==PAGE:99==
CHAPTER 5
Control of a Simple Water System
5.1
Introduction
Now that a simple and valid for control purposes model for the water dynamics has been obtained, it is
time to implement MPC control for a simple water system that initially consists of just one reach. After the
successful implementation for this simple system, the expansion for the case of two reaches and, in the next
chapter, for the full system, with all its different elements, will follow. The ﬁrst goal of this chapter is to prove
that unconstrained MPC without anticipation (i.e. when its horizon is composed of elements of the current
sampling instant only) is essentially the same as LQR when its feedback gain is computed after iterating
backwards in time the discrete time Riccati equation Np times. Subsequently, when anticipation capability is
included in the formulation of MPC, its advantages should start becoming obvious. The next step constitutes
of the introduction of hard constrained MPC while the hard input constraints will be translated into saturation
values for LQR. The comparison of hard constrained MPC and LQR will reveal interesting ﬁndings for the
case where physical actuator constraints need to be taken into consideration. As it has already been stated,
when constraints are taken into consideration, soft MPC should always be implemented in order to avoid
infeasibility that can cause serious problems to the system. Henceforth, soft MPC should be developed,
implemented and tested in this chapter.
5.2
Single Reach River System
With the help of Figure 5.1, the reader can understand better the different components of the single reach
river system under examination here. The disturbance inﬂow, qin, can be seen with red at the upstream point
while the control variable and outﬂow of the system, qout1, can be seen with blue at the downstream point.
Finally, the controlled variable, which is the water level at the end of the reach, hR1 (denoted as Height 1 in
the ﬁgure), can be seen with cyan color. Note that even if the intermediate water levels are not controlled
their behaviour was checked back in Chapter 4 for the single reach. Since outﬂows from each reach will be
constrained due to actuators (gates) operational limits, similar behaviour should be expected for the reaches
of the full system, while it is assumed that these values do not cause ﬂooding. However, it goes without
saying that in case of an extreme disturbance water levels of the uniform ﬂow part will be directly affected
as Figure 4.16 suggests and consequently maybe ﬂoods cannot be avoided in this part.
In addition, the prediction and control horizon for all the controllers is selected to be Np = Nu =
50 samples = 13.88 hours. Actually, this choice was a product of trade off. This means that a large horizon
is necessary in order the MPC to have enough time at its disposal to react adequately before a disturbance
or reference change actually occurs while at the same time it leads to relatively high computation times.
69
==PAGE:100==
70
CHAPTER 5. CONTROL OF A SIMPLE WATER SYSTEM
Figure 5.1: Schematic representation of the single reach system.
Note that this value of the prediction horizon is considered to be high enough with respect to the system’s
dynamics in order to ensure stability. Besides, Table 5.1 includes all the numerical values of the constraints
used in the following simulations where hR1s is the stationary value of the water level at the end of the reach
which was found in Equation 4.15 to be equal to hR1s = 17.11 m. This water level actually represents the
water level at the ninth discretization point referring to discretized Saint Vevant equations (remember that 8
cells are used for the discretization in space of each reach). Ultimately, it should not be forgotten that the
control actions are calculated based on the ID model but applied on the non-linear Saint Venant equations
from which the measurements are also obtained. Note additionally that the reach that will be treated here has
the parameters presented in Table 4.1.

5.2.1
Unconstrained MPC
First of all, the term unconstrained implies that there are no constraints for the system to be taken into
consideration and as it makes sense it is not the most frequent case. As stated in [14], when the prediction
horizon, Np, approaches inﬁnity and there are no constraints and no anticipation, MPC becomes the well
known LQR whose constant feedback law can be calculated by Equation 3.20 after the determination of
P d∞using Equation 3.19. Nevertheless, if the prediction horizon is not very long, as in this case, then in
==PAGE:101==
5.2. SINGLE REACH RIVER SYSTEM
71
order to obtain the same results, the following Riccati Equation:
P d(k) = Qd +AT
d P d(k+1)Ad −AT
d P d(k+1)Bd(Rd +BT
d P d(k+1)Bd)−1BT
d P d(k+1)Ad (5.1)
has to be iterated backwards Np times using the relation P d(k + Np) = Qd as ﬁnal condition. Equation 5.1
is obtained by applying the matrix inversion lemma on Equation 3.15. Afterwards, using Equation 3.20, the
constant discrete LQR gain matrix is calculated.
In all the following cases, it is assumed that the current state vector of the process is known at every
time step. More speciﬁcally, the water levels are measured at each time sample whereas the other
states are either delayed versions of the known disturbance or of the calculated control signal and can
thus be calculated. Additionally, it should not be forgotten that it is assumed that there is a perfect
knowledge of the disturbances affecting the system for all the following simulations.
Furthermore, it has to be reminded that as the strategy of Section 3.4.3 is followed also for LQR, the system
has now 4 states; two in order to incorporate the delay time affecting the input disturbance ﬂow, qin, one for
the water level at the end of the reach and ﬁnally the last one for the control variable, qout1. The state space
model of the single reach system can be found in Appendix B.1. According to the guidelines of Section
3.4.3 and more speciﬁcally Equations 3.29 and 3.32 the new matrix describing the controlled outputs of the
system becomes:

Qd,1 = 10 · CT

Rd,1 = 1
(5.3)
with Qd,1 ∈R4×4 and Rd,1 ∈R1×1. Obviously, the performance is optimized with respect to the water
level at the end of the reach. It is important to mention that the larger the values of Rd,1 compared to the
elements of Qd,1, the smaller the changes in the control action will be. In other words, the controller will
react slower and less aggressively than a controller with smaller elements in Rd,1 compared to the elements
of Qd,1. Howbeit, a less aggressive controller will result to less wear on the actuators of the system while
undoubtedly, it is more robust regarding modelling errors. Therefore, a trade off between how fast is the
controller and the lifetime of the actuators should always be the case. The next step is the implementation of
the procedure explained in Section 3.3.5 as the goal here is the water level at the end of the reach to follow a
given reference.
In order the perform a valid comparison, unconstrained MPC should use the same cost function as LQR and
so:

In ﬁrst place, a reference change test is conducted and as it can clearly be observed in the two plots on
the left column of Figure 5.2, the unconstrained MPC without anticipation and the standard LQR produce
identical results. Moreover, the responses of the controlled variables lie on top whilst the calculated control
signals from the controllers lie on the bottom. The argumentation that the behaviours of the system with the
two different controllers are indeed identical can also be veriﬁed by the results on Table 5.2.
Besides, on the right of the same Figure, the results of a simulation where MPC has the capability of
anticipation, are depicted. Obviously, in this case, MPC is seen to react well in advance so that its cost
function is minimized through the whole horizon while LQR reacts only when the reference change actually
occurs. In fact, although both controllers are able to steer the water levels to the reference value, MPC
exploits its anticipation capability in order to reach the reference value sooner than LQR whereas the control
action demand is smaller. Even if the costs on Table 5.2 show a reduction for both performance indexes
for the case of MPC with anticipation, its real advantage will be shown later when the constraints of the
system are taken into consideration. It is apparent that red color is used in Table 5.2 in order to show the
increase of the performance indexes, with respect to MPC with anticipation for the case of standard LQR
and MPC without anticipation. In addition, an important comment that should be made is that as stated in
Section 3.3.5, model mismatch results to a not perfect reference tracking from the LQR. Here and at the
following plots, differences at the third decimal digit are noticed between reference and actual water level
for LQR; something that reveals that the used ID model is indeed accurate enough.

5.2.2
Hard Constrained MPC
Undisputedly, the vast majority of systems have constraints on their inputs and outputs that have to be taken
into account and river systems are typically such systems. Classical control methods like standard LQR
cannot incorporate these constraints directly into their formulation oppositely to MPC where these constraints
constitute a part of its optimization as presented back in Chapter 3. More speciﬁcally, the outﬂow of the
reach that is controlled, should be limited and of course not negative (at least a small positive ﬂow should
always be guaranteed for environmental reasons) while at the same time its rate of change is also constrained
in order to protect the equipment (gates) from excessive wear. Except from the actuator constraints, there
are of course output constraints that have to be respected. This means for this case that water level at the
end of the reach should always be between some limits. The upper limit is used in order to avoid ﬂooding
conditions while the lower one in order to prevent low water level conditions that can be environmentally
harmful. Nevertheless, these constraints can be changed according to the speciﬁcations of the problem whilst
the existing constraints for this reach can be seen back in Table 5.1. The used weight matrices are:
Qz,1 = 10,
R1 = 1
(5.6)
while in order to create the matrices needed for the inequalities in Equation 3.69 the following matrix is
==PAGE:105==
5.2. SINGLE REACH RIVER SYSTEM
75
used:
Czdc,1 =

0
0
1
0
0
0
0
1


(5.7)
A reference change comparing hard constrained MPC and saturated LQR can be seen in Figure 5.5. It can
be noticed that here the reference change is bigger compared to this of Figure 5.2. However, again, the
difference between the two control methods is the fact that MPC starts moving the water level towards the
new reference value earlier than LQR. For this speciﬁc test, LQR is not saturating and both can steer the
water levels to the reference values. Despite this fact, other tests can have much different results as the
saturation can produce unpredicted results for the system. Even so, it is obvious that the control action
for LQR presents some sharp changes (LQR reacts when reference change actually happens) that can be
harmful for the equipment (gates for example). In addition, the performance indexes associated with this
simulation follow the same pattern as for the unconstrained case since there is no constraints violation. The
only purpose of this test was for completeness of the simulation scenarios.
Figure 5.5: Reference change test for the single reach with LQR and hard constrained MPC.
The harmful effects that saturation may have for the system can be seen in Figure 5.6, in which a disturbance
change scenario (Figure 5.3) is simulated. In particular, it is shown that MPC manages to reduce the deviation
from the setpoint much more than LQR with saturation does. This can be seen additionally on Table 5.4
where the difference in the performance indicators is now really noticeable. Apparently, neither MPC nor
LQR can steer the water level exactly back to the reference value when the large positive disturbance takes
place as the control input, for both cases, reaches the constraint value. However, for MPC, if the weight
matrices have been selected differently and more speciﬁcally if R1 was chosen even smaller compared to
Qz,1 then the reference value would be tracked accurately at the expense of more sharp changes for the rate
of change of control action. Thereafter, the tuning of the controllers should always be closely connected
with the speciﬁcations of each simulation scenario.
In addition, concerning the rate of change of the ﬂow for LQR, it should be pointed out that almost
immediately after the positive change of disturbance, it reaches the saturation value whilst afterwards it goes
==PAGE:106==
76
CHAPTER 5. CONTROL OF A SIMPLE WATER SYSTEM
to zero as soon as the control ﬂow reaches the maximum allowed value. Apparently, LQR activates the
existing constraints both for the rate of change of the ﬂow and the ﬂow itself resulting to a more intense use
of the actuators. Furthermore, although at this test the water level does not exceed the upper hard constraint,
which might represent a ﬂood limit, a more severe disturbance can easily lead to violation of these boundaries
for LQR as it is not aware of the existence of the constraints. Obviously, this can lead to devastating results
for the river system. With the aid of this test it becomes crystal clear why MPC is selected as the control
strategy that will be implemented when dealing with the full river system in the next chapter. However,
standard LQR will be simulated again for the double reach system just for completeness.

Finally, for soft constrained MPC, it is decided that the output constraints will be implemented as soft

also:
Sn,1 = ypen
(5.8)
where ypen = 1015, referring to Equation 3.78. It is evident that the penalty for the slack variables is very
high compared to the weight used for the penalization of the deviation of the output from the reference value,
==PAGE:107==
5.2. SINGLE REACH RIVER SYSTEM
77
Qz,1. The reason is that it is requested that slack variables are activated only when it is absolutely necessary
(avoidance of infeasibility). This forces the controller to keep the violations of the soft constraints as small
as possible or if possible equal to zero when a large disturbance takes place . Additionally, ¯F , referring to
Equation 3.84, is manipulated accordingly in order to include slack variables only for the water level and not
for the ﬂow (control variable). This makes sense as each actuator has some operational limits that in case of
violation may lead to failure of the equipment. Thereby, referring to Equations 3.86, it is obtained that:

In order to run a test where infeasibility is a real threat, the hard constraints were selected tighter than
these of Table 5.1. Hence, now hmax = hR1s + 0.8 m and hmin = hR1s −0.8 m are selected as (soft)
constraints for the water level at the end of the reach. Additionally, the maximum ﬂow is constrained to
be 70 m3/s. Again the disturbance depicted in Figure 5.3 is applied and the result is that soft MPC can
effectively handle extreme conditions by violating the existing output constraint. More speciﬁcally, in Figure
5.7 the slack variables together with the output of the system can be seen. It has to be highlighted that only
the value of the slack variables predicted at the ﬁrst time instant of the horizon are plotted at each time stamp.
Evidently, even if MPC predicts the positive disturbance change and lowers the water level, this action is
not adequate and subsequently maximum slack variable should be activated in order to avoid infeasibility
while the minimum slack variable stays constantly equal to zero. Apart from the large disturbance and the
tight constraints of the output, another reason behind the activation of the slack variables is the existence of
the constraints regarding the control variable which are depicted in Figure 5.8. Naturally, one may wonder
why the rate of change of the input remains smaller than the maximum constraint and does not help to avoid
violation of the constraints. The answer is connected with the prediction horizon which is approximately
13.88 hours as already said. Hence, when MPC sees that a violation of the soft constraint will take place,
control input has already reached the maximum value and consequently violation becomes inevitable. Note
that if the same simulation was performed under hard constrained MPC scheme, it would lead to infeasibility
since it would be impossible for the optimizer to return a solution respecting the existing constraints.

horizon during a disturbance change test for the single reach with soft constrained MPC.

are the ﬂows qin and qlat. The last one could represent a discharge due to the demand of a customer or a
small river that takes water from the main one and it is located at the backwater area of the second reach.

be very close to this of the ﬁrst reach. This was also expected since the two backwater areas have almost
the same width (actually since the backwater area of the ﬁrst reach begins at points were the width is
larger compared to the starting point of the backwater area of the second reach, the mean width of the ﬁrst
backwater area is a bit larger than the second one) and as explained before, approximately the same length. It
is repeated that all the calculated control actions are fed to the nonlinear Saint Venant equations. In addition,
the same constraints as these shown in Table 5.1 apply for the second reach except from the water level
constraints which is constrained to be always inside the limits hR2s + 0.77 m and hR2s −0.77 m, where
hR2s = 11.597 m according to Equation 5.10.

the disturbance change and creates buffer capacity by accordingly lowering or increasing the water levels
before the actual disturbance change affects the system. An interesting remark is that at 50 hours, LQR

==PAGE:113==
5.3. DOUBLE REACH RIVER SYSTEM
83
control input for the ﬁrst and second reach are depicted, respectively. Certainly, despite the fact that none
of the controllers is able to steer the water levels accurately back to the reference values, MPC presents, in
total, smaller deviations from the reference values. Additionally, it is evident that, similarly to the case of the
single reach, due to actuator constraints, LQR produces saturated rate of change for the control inputs and
saturated control inputs. Apparently, this fact together with the anticipation capability of MPC are the causes
behind the big differences regarding the costs presented in Table 5.8. One interesting ﬁnding is that during
the positive disturbance change in qin, a water level lower than the reference value is obtained for the second
reach when LQR is employed. The reason is that, initially, although qout1 and qout2 are equal (due to the
constraints regarding the rate of change of the ﬂow), qout1 needs a time equal to the time delay to reach the
backwater area of the second reach. Afterwards, qout1 always demands to be higher from qout2 in order to
correct this deviation but due to saturation this cannot be implemented leading to a constant deviation from
the reference value. It is undeniable, of course, that if a more aggressive setup was the choice for MPC (that
means that more sharp changes are allowed for ∆u) then reference value would be tracked accurately but
with the price of a faster wear of the equipment. Finally, even if here hard constrains regarding the water
levels are not violated neither for the ﬁrst nor for the second reach when LQR is used, different disturbance
changes can result to devastating results since LQR is not aware of the existing output constraints.

5.4
Conclusion
The target of this chapter was not only to control a simple water system that consists of one or two reaches,
but also to reveal the superiority of MPC when compared to standard LQR. Firstly, it was successfully shown
that unconstrained MPC without anticipation is identical to LQR. The strong advantages of MPC started
to emerge when unconstrained MPC with anticipation was used and they became crystal clear when hard
constraints were introduced. The only way to impose these hard constraints on LQR was with the use of
saturation. In addition, the introduction of slack variables allowed to deal with extreme conditions (large
==PAGE:116==
86
CHAPTER 5. CONTROL OF A SIMPLE WATER SYSTEM
Figure 5.17: Water level at the end of the second reach and values of the slack variables for the ﬁrst
prediction in the horizon during a disturbance change test for the double reach system with soft constrained
MPC.
Figure 5.18: Control input and rate of change of control input for the second reach during a disturbance
change test for the double reach system with soft constrained MPC.
disturbances with tight hard constraints regarding the water levels) without ending up with an infeasible
problem. All the above tests were repeated for a double reach river system and the conclusion was again
that MPC, mainly due to its capability to incorporate the existing constraints and the anticipation for future
disturbances and reference changes, gives better results and therefore should be chosen as the control method
when dealing with the extended full river system.
==PAGE:117==
CHAPTER 6
MPC for an Extended Water System
6.1
Introduction
After the fruitful implementation of MPC and LQR for a simple river system, which veriﬁed the strong
points of MPC, an extended river system will be treated including, except from river reaches, two lakes,
a turbine and a pump. However, as the system becomes more complicated the requested time in order to
conduct the optimizations starts becoming a threatening problem. Hence, it is of vital importance to search
for efﬁcient ways to reduce the computational load. Actually, the way that slack variables are handled can
give a signiﬁcant reduction regarding the computational load but the cost on the performance of MPC that
each method has, should also be carefully examined. Therefore, suitable performance indexes should be
introduced in order to be able to compare efﬁciently the different strategies and decide which one should be
implemented resulting to relaxation of computational load without obtaining a signiﬁcantly worse behaviour
of the system. Towards this direction, appropriate simulation scenarios should be created and executed.
6.2
Extended Water System
As it was declared in the introduction of this thesis, the extended water system treated here is inspired by [62]
and is depicted in Figure 6.1. This system, compared to this depicted in Figure 5.9, includes, additionally,
two more river reaches and two lakes. Moreover, one of the lakes (Lake 2) communicates with the river with
a turbine and a pump station whilst the outﬂow out of the other one (Lake 1) is a control variable that can be
manipulated with an hydraulic structure (gate for example). Apart from that, it is assumed that the turbine
and pump discharges can be controlled directly (i.e. having a local controller) while the pump extracts water
from the backwater area of Reach 1 and the turbine feeds with water the backwater area of Reach 2. It
should also be pointed out that the water level at the end of the fourth reach is not controlled. However, it is
assumed that there is a weir at the end of the fourth reach whose width is equal to the width of the end of the
reach, wweir = 100m, and its height is set to be equal to pweir = 13.55m. Apparently, since the weir has a
ﬁxed height that is not adjustable there is no way to control, at this point, the ﬂow out of the river. Note that
pumps, turbines and weirs were introduced theoretically back in Section 2.6.
Thereafter, the controlled variables now are the water levels at the end of the ﬁrst three reaches along with
the water levels of the lakes (cyan color). In addition, the control variables are the ﬂows through the three
gates together with the ﬂow of the turbine and the pump and the outﬂow from Lake 1 (blue color). Finally,
concerning the disturbances, except from the lake inﬂows, qinlake1 and qinlake2, there are also lateral
off-takes at the end of the second, third and fourth reach, qlat1, qlat2 and qlat3, respectively, and a lateral

Figure 6.1: Extended water system that consists of a river of four reaches, two lakes, a turbine and a pump.
By investigating the aforementioned stationary values, it can be concluded that the third reach, which is
very steep and has a relatively narrow end, has a long part of uniform ﬂow (approximately 7.56 km) and a
short (approximately 3.44 km) and narrow backwater area. So, a high time delay should be the case for this
reach. As far as the fourth reach is concerned, it is relatively ﬂat and this is veriﬁed by the results which give
a longer (approximately 6.5 km ) and narrower (compared to Reach 3) backwater area and a short part of
uniform ﬂow (approximately 1.5 km). Therefore, a small time delay is expected.
Following the guidelines presented back in Section 4.4 and always remembering that the most challenging
case is that of low ﬂow condition, the selected number of delay time steps are set to be kd3 = 4 (time delay is
equal to 4000s) and kd4 = 1 (time delay is equal to 1000s) while the backwater areas are set, after excessive
tuning, to be As3 = 390 ∗103 m2 and As4 = 490.5 ∗103 m2 for the third and fourth reach, respectively.
Evidently, compared to this of Reach 3, the backwater area of Reach 4 is larger and so it can be deducted that
the increase of the length had a more profound impact than the decrease of the width of the backwater area.
Regarding the model employed for the lakes, as proposed in [62], if the water inﬂow and outﬂow of the lake
under consideration are denoted as qinlake and qoutlake respectively, then the volume of the water in the
lake, υlake, changes accordingly to the following relation:

6.3. IMPLEMENTATION OF SOFT CONSTRAINED MPC

The reason behind the choice to include the ﬂows through the turbine and the pump as well as the delayed
version of the ﬂow out of Reach 3 in the controlled variables vector will become clear in the next section. Note
that dots are used for space purposes and indicate that the vector continues on this direction. Furthermore,
regarding the notation, in 6.7, the number after the comma reveals the number of steps of delay. Besides, the
matrices of the state space model for the extended water system can be found at Appendix B.3.
6.3
Implementation of Soft Constrained MPC
Unquestionably, one major difference after the inclusion of the pump and the turbine is that special care
should be given in order to avoid their pointless activation at steady state condition. That is, when the turbine
extracts the same amount of water as the pump adds to Lake 2. This happens in situations where all the
water levels have reached their reference values and any effort to bring qt and qc back to zero will mean an
increase in the cost function since during the formulation of MPC, it has been selected to penalize ∆u (see
Equations 3.49 and 6.8). Consequently, in order to ensure that these control ﬂows are activated only when
they are really needed, penalization of their deviation from zero should be added. However, it goes without
saying that this weight should be relatively small in order to allow the use of the turbine and the pump when
it is needed while remaining at zero, otherwise. Therefore, the reformulated cost function is:
min
∆U, n φ =
Np
X
i=1
∥zfulli −ri∥2
Qz,full +
Np−1
X
i=0
∥∆ui∥2
Rfull +
Np
X
i=1
∥ni∥Sn,full
(6.8)
The vector ri contains the reference values for all signals in zfull. When the matrices for the optimization
are formed, the same tactic as explained in Chapter 3, is followed. Note that the reference signal for the
delayed version of the control ﬂow out of reach 3, qout3,1, is formed appropriately in order to satisfy the
demand of a set of customers given in qlat3 (i.e. qlat3 cannot extract stored water from the backwater area).
Therefore, it is requested that qout3,1 follows a reference so that the demand is satisﬁed as well as possible.
In addition, as it is desired that there is a a minimum ﬂow of 5 m3/s in each reach, the reference that should
be followed from qout3,1 when the lateral off-take is zero, is set to be 5 m3/s so that the ﬂow out of the river
is minimized. Furthermore, the vector for all the variables that are to be constrained, referring to Equation
3.66, are:

(6.10)
The penalization of the deviation of the j-th (j = 1, ..., dim(zfull)) variable in zfull is represented by the
j-th diagonal element of Qz,full. Apparently, the penalization of the water levels in the three reaches are
equally important whilst this of the second lake is penalized more. At the same time, the water level of the
ﬁrst lake is lightly penalized since it has a much bigger capacity than the second one and water is desired
to be stored in it if there is a large disturbance inﬂow, qinlake1. On the other hand, while it is desired to
track accurately the reference for qout3,1, its deviation is not heavily penalized so that the whole system can
extract water in case of a severe disturbance change. Furthermore, the deviation of the turbine ﬂow from
zero is penalized very lightly so that it is allowed to be activated when it is needed whilst the pump ﬂow is
penalized more so that it is activated only when it is absolutely necessary. By observing Rfull, it can be
seen that the rates of change regarding the ﬂow through the turbine and the pump are penalized more than
the other control signals so that they respond more smoothly. Last but not least, it should be mentioned
that the prediction horizon used in the following simulations is Np = 50 samples = 13.88 hours and the
calculated control signals are applied to the nonlinear system.
6.4
Comparison of Different Methods Concerning Slack Variables
In Section 3.4.6, the use of the slack variables was introduced in order to avoid infeasibility. The main
drawback of using slack variables is that they result to a raise of the computation time needed for the
optimization, which of course has to be as small as possible, since the number of decision variables is
increased signiﬁcantly. As a matter of fact, when dealing with the extended river system it becomes apparent
that the time needed for the execution of the simulations soars noticeably compared to the single or double
reach system due to the higher number of states. For this reason, various ways to implement soft constraints
with different amount of slack variables will be examined and compared with the aid of two illustrative
simulations. Similarly to the cases of single and double reach systems, again the water levels of the reaches
together with these of the lakes are softened i.e. ns = 5. So, it is important to notice that here not all
the controlled variables are softened like for the cases of the single and double reach systems as the ﬂows
through the pump and the turbine remain hard constrained. Of course, qout3,1 is indirectly constrained since
it constitutes a delayed version of the hard constrained qout3.
==PAGE:123==
6.4. COMPARISON OF DIFFERENT METHODS CONCERNING SLACK VARIABLES

6.4.1
Method 1
The initial and most computationally demanding version is when the full slack vector is used. To be more
speciﬁc, different slack variables have to be used for each state that has to be softened, for each time instant
in the whole prediction horizon and also for violation of the maximum and minimum constraints. This
results to a number of different slack variables in the case of the system under investigation that is equal to
Nslack = 2Npns = 500. The matrices for the optimization as shown in Equations 3.84, 3.86 and 3.87, can
be deﬁned as:

Scenario 1
The ﬁrst scenario concerns a change in the disturbance that enters Lake 2, qinlake2, and in the lateral intake
of the river, qinlat. The simulated scenario is depicted in Figure 6.2. Moreover, Figure 6.3 presents the water
level of Lake 2, hlake2, Figure 6.4 shows the water level at the end of Reach 2, hR2, and, ﬁnally, Figure 6.5
depicts the water ﬂow through the turbine, qt, for all the different methods. Since Method 2 gives identical
results compared to Method 1, the plots regarding this method are omitted for space reasons. Note that the
plots concerning the water levels at the end of Reach 1, 3 and at Lake 1 (hR1, hR3 and hlake1) together with
==PAGE:127==
6.4. COMPARISON OF DIFFERENT METHODS CONCERNING SLACK VARIABLES
97
plots about the control actions and their rate of change can be found in Appendix C.1. Note that when the
different tactics do not give identical results, different plots are used in order to give a clearer view. Next,
Figure 6.6 shows how slack variables are predicted to be activated at the ﬁrst time instant of the prediction
horizon, for each method. Apart from that, interesting deductions about the various methods of handling the
slack variables can be made with the aid of Figures 6.7 and 6.8 where histograms of the demanded time
to perform each optimization (red lines show the mean value) and the memory allocated from the system,
are depicted, respectively. In addition, Table 6.4 contains the six aforementioned performance indexes.
Percentages in parentheses are used to indicate the deviation with respect to Method 1.
Obviously, the violation of the softened constraints connected with Lake 2 and Reach 2 is inevitable for all
the different methods since, although MPC foresees the upcoming disturbance, the decrease in the water
levels is far from enough to face the large disturbance. Interestingly, from roughly 130hours to 157hours the
turbine ﬂow is decreased. The reason is that during this period Reach 2 has to face the disturbance change in
qinlat. Actually, the amount of decrease in turbine ﬂow is approximately the same as the change in qinlat.
Furthermore, Methods 1,2 and 3, which use different slack variables for the ﬁrst time instant in the horizon
and different for the rest of the time instants, can apparently use less the slack variables corresponding to
the ﬁrst time instant since it is possible to activate them later compared to Methods 4 and 5 that use the
same slack variable for the whole horizon (see Figure 6.6). Apart from that, when Method 5, where just one
common slack variable is employed for all the softened states, is used, unnecessarily large violations are
the case. To be more speciﬁc, as both water levels at Reach 2 and Lake 2 use the same slack variable, they
are both allowed to violate their soft constraints the same (approximately 2.25 m is the maximum common
allowed violation) whilst all the other methods handle separately each softened controlled variable.
A consequence of this behaviour is the oscillations regarding the ﬂow of the turbine which connects Lake 2
and Reach 2. In particular, approximately at around 190 hours after the start of the simulation, when both
the water levels at Lake 2 and Reach 2 start to decrease, qt presents a rather oscillatory behaviour (see Figure
6.5). The reason is that the turbine tries to minimize the maximum violation (either this of the lake or of the
reach) using the turbine, accordingly. However, afterwards, the other violation becomes larger and thus the
turbine ﬂow is acting now the opposite way (decreasing or increasing the ﬂow from the lake to the reach).
This pattern continues until both water levels have entered inside the region set by their soft constraints
moving towards their reference values. This phenomenon also leads the slack variable to be deactivated a bit
later compared to the other strategies. Similarly, for Method 4, it is apparent that after roughly 190 hours
only the slack variable of Lake 2 starts to decrease whereas the respective of Reach 2 starts to decline only
when it becomes larger than this of Lake 2. In addition, for Method 3, the fact that it uses different slack
variables for future instants allows the beginning of the decrease of the slack variable of Reach 2 slightly
before it becomes larger than the slack variable of Lake 2. Ultimately, Method 1 has the best results since
both slack variables start to decrease simultaneously and are deactivated together.
As far as the computation time is concerned, it is evident that the smaller is the number of slack variables,
the smaller is the mean time of optimization execution. By examining Figure 6.7, it can be seen that the
optimization execution time does not follow a speciﬁc distribution. Similar deductions can be made with the
help of Figure 6.8 where it is apparent that a larger amount of slack variables lead to a more intense use of
the memory of the system. Conclusively, despite the fact that methods that use different slack variables for
the ﬁrst time instant of the prediction horizon and different for the future time instants seem to handle better
the violation of soft constraints, the computational burden they result to is reasonably larger. In order to have
==PAGE:128==
98

a broader view, Scenario 2 that follows, concerns a test where no violation of soft constraints takes place. As
a matter of fact, this is the most common case.

6.4. COMPARISON OF DIFFERENT METHODS CONCERNING SLACK VARIABLES

Scenario 2
The simulation scenario can be seen in Appendix C.2 together with the plots concerning the water levels,
the control variables and their rate of change. Brieﬂy, the scenario concerns reference changes in hR1 and
hR2 together with changes in qlat3 and qinlake2. As it is totally expected, the responses of the 5 different
methods are identical, since slack variables remain always deactivated, and, consequently, the attention
comes to the computational load of each method. Hence, the histograms in Figure 6.9 depict the time that
each optimization needs in order to be executed. Comparing it with Figure 6.7 it can clearly be seen that the
time of optimization execution follows a distribution that can be approximated by the normal one, oppositely
to the case of violation. In addition, Table 6.5 reveals that when no violation happens in the system then
moving from Method 1 to Method 5 leads to a similar reduction in computation time compared to the case of
violation. Of course, averagely, the optimization needs more time to be conducted when there is violation of
the soft constraints. Apart from that, comparing Figures 6.8 and 6.10, it can be deducted that memory usage
follows the same pattern regarding the two scenarios (values and percentages of reduction moving from
Method 1 to 5, are very close) since the size of the used matrices plays the most crucial role concerning the
consumption of memory. Small discrepancies between the allocated memory for the two scenarios probably
can be addressed to the different scenario conﬁguration.

Discussion of the Results
It is without doubt that each method of handling the slack variables has its strong and weak points. More
speciﬁcally, although methods that use different slack variables for the ﬁrst and for the rest time instants in
the prediction horizon (Method 1, 2 and 3) are able to handle better a severe violation of the slack variables,
they result to more computationally intense calculations. On the other hand, Method 4 that uses the same
slack variables for the whole horizon results to bigger violations but the optimization is performed much
faster using less memory. Finally, the last method that employs only one slack variable for all the softened
controlled outputs produces the worst results in terms of soft constraints violation.
Moreover, since the majority of the cases concerns situations where no violation of the soft constraints
happens and thereby the computational burden becomes the only concern (since the responses of the different
methods are identical), Method 4 is selected as the implemented one at Chapter 7. This is because it was
found to achieve approximately 33% reduction in the mean needed time for the optimization execution and
17% reduction in the memory used by the system, for the case of no violation, with respect to Method 1.
When there was violation of the existing constraints, the decrease in the mean optimization conduction
time was 36% and 17% less memory was used with the price of only 7% more violation of the softened
constraints, with respect to Method 1. Undeniably, different scenarios result to different percentages for the
performance indexes but the general pattern would be the same. Finally, it should be mentioned that if the
system speciﬁcations are different (for example every violation of the softened constraints is very important
and therefore violation should avoided at any cost) the ﬁrst three methods are more suitable.
6.5
Conclusion
This chapter had two essential targets to accomplish; the ﬁst one was to establish the extended water system
treated at this thesis and afterwards to develop and test various methods of handling the slack variables. After
the formulation of the optimization problem for the extended system, ﬁve different methods of manipulating
the slack variables were examined. After the execution of two different simulation scenarios (one with
violation of some soft constraints and one without) and using six performance indicators, revealing ﬁndings
were obtained whilst based on these deductions a selection was made regarding the implemented method of
handling the soft constraints in the following chapter.
==PAGE:133==
CHAPTER 7
EMPC for an Extended Water System
7.1
Introduction
After the design of the extended water system and the formulation of MPC for this system, with the aid of
an efﬁcient and effective way of handling the slack variables, EMPC, that was treated theoretically back in
Section 3.5 will be developed and tested in this chapter. The ﬁrst step will be to appropriately formulate
the optimization problem regarding the operation of the river system directly with respect to its economics.
Afterwards, an appropriate scenario should be created in order to clearly demonstrate the potential of EMPC.
To be more speciﬁc, the target is to illustrate how EMPC exploits the information about the prices of the
inputs and the outputs of the system and consequently how it changes its decisions accordingly in order to
maximize the economic proﬁt. Afterwards, the way that EMPC handles the customers’ orders for water will
be investigated whereas a comparison with standard MPC will be the case in order to better understand the
differences between these two approaches.
7.2
EMPC Formulation
As far as the extended water system treated at this thesis is concerned, the objective is to maximize the
proﬁt by following the pattern of pumping water in the lake when the electricity price is low while using
the turbine when the electricity price is high. Nevertheless, it is also of high interest to minimize the total
outﬂow from the river in order to achieve a wiser exploitation of water resources. Thus, the water running
out of the river should also be included in the cost function. In addition, various approaches have been used
for the deﬁnition and prediction of accurate prices of the electricity power. Nonetheless, this is beyond the
scope of this project. Moreover, to use realistic prices for electricity and water does not help to reveal the
performance of EMPC because of the fact that there is a big gap of prices for the operation of the speciﬁc
system. That is, if the water is too expensive with respect to electricity power price, EMPC decides not
to produce any power so that the outﬂow is minimized, whilst if it is too cheap, EMPC produces as much
power as possible and does not give any importance to the minimization of the total outﬂow. Henceforth,
artiﬁcial unit prices will be used in order to demonstrate the decisions this control technique produces in
different modes (different price scenarios). Generally speaking, for a river system operator these prices may
incorporate various factors except from economic proﬁt like environmental factors and actuators wear, to
name but a few. Imagine for example the case of an extremely dry period during which, even though the
price of energy does not change signiﬁcantly, water becomes really important and its preservation becomes a
major issue that should be reﬂected in the cost function of EMPC.

==PAGE:134==

Evidently, the minimization of the cost function in 7.1 yields the maximization of the proﬁt through power
generation and the minimization of the water that ﬂows out of the river. As it has already been mentioned,
the prediction horizon here has to be selected long enough, so that the operation of the system can take
advantage of the low electricity prices, which are a result of lower demand, storing water in the lake and
can exploit the turbine during the periods that electricity price is generally higher due to increased demand.
Unquestionably, a long horizon should be the case also in order to guarantee stability as explained in Section
3.5.3.

programming (LP) optimization can be executed with the aid of the function linprog, which performs the
optimization:
