Apparently, EMPC formulation described by Equations 7.8, 7.9 and 7.10 has the form of Equations 7.3
and 7.4. Moreover, the difference between standard MPC and EMPC becomes evident by comparing the
respective cost functions (see Equations 6.8 and 7.1). To be more speciﬁc, while MPC is designed in order
to penalize deviations from some given set-points, EMPC minimizes an economic cost directly. Therefore,
now that EMPC has been formulated, it is time to set up suitable scenarios in order to reveal its potential.
7.3
Simulation Scenario 1 for EMPC
First and foremost, before the formulation of the simulation scenario, it should be underlined that some
assumptions have been made in order to simplify the problem and focus more on the concept of EMPC.
To be more speciﬁc, as expressed in Equations 2.44 and 2.46, the electrical power produced by the turbine
and absorbed by the pump, respectively, depends, except from the constants kt and kc, also on the turbine
and pump head. This means that the water levels of the Reach 1, Reach 2 and Lake 2 should also be taken
into consideration in order to calculate the power production and absorption. Nevertheless, since the water
levels of the reaches and the lakes are small compared to the ﬁxed height of the duct of the turbine and of
the pump (see Table 6.1) and additionally are constrained to remain inside some relatively tight limits, it
can be assumed that turbine and pump heads remain constant during the simulation scenario. Thereby, it is
valid to assume that the power produced by the turbine and absorbed by the pump is proportional to the ﬂow
through them and conduct the optimization using prices related directly to the ﬂows. In addition, it has to be
==PAGE:137==
7.3. SIMULATION SCENARIO 1 FOR EMPC
107
underlined again that the prices used in the simulation are unit prices that do not represent real values. It
goes without saying that when it comes to real systems these prices can incorporate a lot of factors that affect
the operation of a river system (economic gain, environmental factors e.t.c). Moreover, the constraints used
for the following scenario are presented in Table 7.1 while the established state space model is illustrated in
Appendix B.4. Note that, again, nonlinear simulations are performed.

Table 7.1: Constraints of the extended water system for Scenario 1 of EMPC.
The ﬁrst simulation scenario will treat the case of changes in the price corresponding to the ﬂows of the
turbine and the pump as well as in the price of the water that ﬂows out of the river. Clearly, pump ﬂow
price has been selected to be always 10% more expensive than this of the turbine ﬂow in order to avoid
meaningless activation of the pump. For example, if the prices were exactly the same then EMPC would
maybe decide to set the turbine ﬂow at 30 m3/s and the pump ﬂow at 10 m3/s instead of just using the
turbine with a ﬂow of 20 m3/s. In parallel, there will also be changes in the disturbances qinlake2 and
qinlat, referring to the schematic representation of the system in Figure 6.1. The prices together with these
disturbances can be seen in Figure 7.1. Lateral offtakes are zero for this scenario. As it is obvious, the
simulation scenario has been divided into 4 parts in order to better illustrate the potential of EMPC. Certainly,
these modes are not totally independent of each other, since the state of the system at the end of one part
inﬂuences the next one. Regarding the aforementioned parts, it can be said that:
• During mode A, it is obvious that a large disturbance qinlake2 starts entering Lake 2 at the commence
of the simulation and lasts roughly 41 hours. Apparently, since EMPC is aware of the price of the
water, it should be able to ofﬂoad this extra amount of water when the price becomes smaller; that is
between approximately 22 hours and 33 hours after the beginning of the simulation scenario.
• During mode B, although the unit price for water remains constant, the price for the ﬂow through
the turbine becomes very high for a time window from 47 hours to 72 hours. Therefore, it would be
desirable that EMPC uses the turbine as much as possible during this time window.
• Mode C may be subdivided into two smaller sections. During the ﬁrst one, from around 83 hours
to 100 hours after the start of the simulation, the prices of the ﬂows of the turbine and the pump are
very low whereas, afterwards, the prices become very high together with a sharp decrease in the water
price. Therefore, it is expected that EMPC will use the pump during the ﬁrst section in order to ﬁll
==PAGE:138==
108
CHAPTER 7. EMPC FOR AN EXTENDED WATER SYSTEM
Figure 7.1: Changes of prices and disturbances for simulation scenario 1 of EMPC.
Lake 2 as much as possible in order to be able later to fully exploit the second section of the mode and
produce as much power as possible with the turbine.
• Ultimately, during mode D, a large disturbance enters the river as qinlat jumps to 45 m3/s from
approximately 135 hours to 155 hours after the beginning of the simulation. EMPC should be able to
see the window of the low price of the water , from 144 hours to 168 hours, and get rid of the excessive
water during this period of time.
With the execution of this scenario, except from the general behaviour of EMPC, the dependency of the
results on the selected elements of Reco and the prediction horizon, Np, should be examined. For this reason,
the simulations will be conducted with three different prediction horizons (Np = 40 = 11.1 hours, Np =
70 = 19.4 hours and Np = 100 = 27.8 hours) whereas:
Reco =
h
3
3
14
28
8
10
iT
× λ
(7.11)
where λ will be selected to have three different values; 0.3, 1 and 3. As it has already been analysed, it is
expected that small values for the elements of Reco will result to a better exploitation of the price changes
but with the cost of a more intense use of the actuators. Similarly, it makes sense that a longer prediction
horizon will bring a better performance but it will also lead to heavier computational burden. In addition, the
elements of Sneco are chosen to be equal to 1015 in order to avoid pointless violation of the soft constrains.
Certainly, again, only the constraints concerning the water levels of the reaches and the lakes have been
softened.
Note that for the following plots, the left side will contain the obtained results for the different values of λ
(prediction horizon is ﬁxed at 70 samples) whilst the right one these for different values of Np (λ ﬁxed at
1). Plots that are not reported here can be found in Appendix C.3. Firstly, Figures 7.2 and 7.3 depict the
water levels of Reach 3 and Lake 2 respectively. Furthermore, Figures 7.4, 7.5 and 7.6 which present the
ﬂow through the turbine, through the pump and out of the river, respectively, can help to better understand
the behaviour of EMPC during this simulation scenario.
==PAGE:139==
7.3. SIMULATION SCENARIO 1 FOR EMPC
109
As a matter of fact, for mode A, it is obvious that the general trend is to use the turbine more actively after
approximately 20 hours in order to compensate for the increase of the disturbance. Before this point, a large
part of the disturbance stays inside the lake resulting to an increasing water level. Evidently, the lower the
values of λ, the sharper change of the turbine ﬂow is noticed when the price of water decreases. Interestingly,
while for λ = 0.3 and λ = 1 the turbine ﬂow goes directly to zero when the price of water increases again,
for λ = 3 this is not the case as any change in the control actions is more heavily penalized. Additionally,
for the smaller prediction horizon, initially, the turbine ﬂow is reduced since it cannot predict that the water
level of Lake 2 will approach the upper constraint and simply tries to minimize the water ﬂowing out of
the system. However, when it does so, EMPC changes sharply the turbine ﬂow to larger values and ﬁnally
ends up with a larger qt compared to the cases of normal and long horizon. Moreover, when long horizon
is employed, the turbine ﬂow does not return to zero after the increase of the water price but holds a small
positive value since MPC foresees that the turbine ﬂow will be increased soon again. Furthermore, Figure
7.6 shows that with long horizon, qout3 is increased substantially during the window of low water price
whereas it is reduced quickly afterwards to avoid expensive prices.
In addition, for mode B, where the desired behaviour is the activation of the turbine, again smaller values of
λ lead to a substantially more extensive use of the turbine. Moreover, with smaller λ the turbine ﬂow sharply
goes to zero when the price of the turbine ﬂow decreases whereas for λ = 1 the drop is performed in a more
smooth way whilst for λ = 3 the ﬂow returns very slowly back to zero. Additionally, when short horizon
is employed, the turbine ﬂow becomes larger compared to normal and long horizon cases. The reason is
that it does not predict that soon it will have to drop back to zero again. Examining the behaviour of qt
for the long horizon it is obvious that very early the turbine ﬂow starts to decline slowly towards zero in
order to avoid sharp changes. For mode C, apparently, smaller λ results to a higher and earlier use of the
pump and consequently to a higher use of the turbine afterwards since the increased water level of Lake 2
allows it. Actually, with λ = 3 the pump remains deactivated and consequently afterwards the turbine can
be lightly used before the water level of Lake 2 reaches the lower constraint. In addition, when short horizon
is employed, EMPC does not predict the economic gain it could have afterwards with the use of the turbine
and consequently pump is not activated and since the water level at Lake 2 remains very close to the lower
constraint, the turbine is hardly used. On the contrary, larger values of the horizon lead to the use of the
pump that allows to rise the water levels of Lake 2 in order to use the turbine afterwards. Undoubtedly, the
long horizon leads to an activation of the pump even before the beginning of mode C. This has as a result the
water level of Lake 2 to reach the maximum constraint and afterwards, when the price is high, the turbine to
be used even more intensively before the water level at Lake 2 reaches the minimum constraint.
Eventually, during mode D, smaller λ results to a larger minimization of the water outﬂow (by increasing
the water level at Reach 3) before the water price decreases (approximately at 145 hours). This is possible
since the water level of Reach 3 had been decreased previously (during mode C) when the water price was
very low. Subsequently, the water that enters the system because of the disturbance change is removed out of
the system more intensively during the window of low price. It is important that when λ = 1 and λ = 3,
qout3 and qout2 do not go directly to the lower constraint when the disturbance stops acting and as a result,
hR2 and hR3 move slowly towards a steady state which is not included in the plots for spacing reasons. The
impact of the different prediction horizon regarding mode D is seen by investigating the case of short horizon
where EMPC does not foresee early enough the water price change and thereby qout3 reacts sharply in order
to face the disturbance change without waiting to fully exploit the window of lower water price. Oppositely,
long horizon exploits more optimally, with respect to its cost function, the windows of low water price both
==PAGE:140==
110
CHAPTER 7. EMPC FOR AN EXTENDED WATER SYSTEM
before and during the disturbance change.
To conclude, it seems like smaller elements for Reco and longer prediction horizon lead to noticeably better
economic performance; something that is actually veriﬁed also by Figure 7.7 where the economic cost index
Jeco is plotted. To be more speciﬁc:
Jeco(k) = −[−cT,kqt(k) + cC,kqc(k) + cW,k(qout3(k) −5)]
(7.12)
where cT,k, cC,k and cW,k represent the cost of the turbine ﬂow, pump ﬂow and outﬂow respectively at time
instant k. Note that ﬂow 5 m3/s is subtracted from the ﬂow out of the river as it constitutes the minimum
constraint regarding the ﬂow through the river. Obviously, Jeco represents the amount of economic proﬁt that
the system makes each time instant whilst the higher positive value is obtained the larger is this proﬁt. As it
makes sense, periods of positive proﬁt are connected with turbine usage whereas periods of low positive or
even negative proﬁt are the case when the system tries to remove extra water out of it or store it in Lake 2.

7.4
Simulation Scenario 2 for EMPC
So far, the operation of the system has been simulated without taking into account the lateral offtakes.
Thereby, the second simulation scenario will deal with this case. As a matter of fact, these offtakes can, in
practice, represent a group of customers in need of water which they order well in advance and is assumed
to be perfectly known during the whole prediction horizon. However, if these demands are included in the
system as known disturbances, that is, the customers order the amount of water they need in advance and they
take all the amount they have ordered, it is not guaranteed that the system can operate normally, respecting
its constraints. In order to overcome this problem, the outﬂows are set as control variables, i.e. although
there are known demands in advance from the customers, MPC decides the water ﬂow it can provide to each
of them so that it ensures that its constraints are respected.
Regarding the simulation scenario, the test should concern extreme cases where the demand ﬂows are large
and with long duration so that it can be seen how the control algorithm handles this situation. Moreover,
it is desired that all the customers are satisﬁed to the same degree. In other words, a group of customers
located at the end of the river should be able to get almost equal amount of water compared to the one in the
beginning of the river, even if the latter one is located close to the areas of water storage. Lastly, the same
scenario will be simulated for the case of standard MPC and Economic MPC with a lateral outﬂows price
drop for a certain amount of time instants in order to see how the two algorithms handle the same problem in
==PAGE:145==
7.4. SIMULATION SCENARIO 2 FOR EMPC
115
a different way. In addition, the turbine and pump ﬂow prices are assumed constant and their behaviour is
not of much interest, as it has been already examined with the aid of the previous simulation scenario. The
point here is to create a simple case that makes the lateral outﬂow demand management crystal clear.
As far as the formulation of the problem is concerned, the system now includes the lateral outﬂows as control
inputs which are then being included in the state vector as explained in Section 3.4.3. Furthermore, it is
desired to minimize the outﬂow from the last reach that is not used by the customers served by the last reach
(so qout3,1(k) −qlat3(k) > 0 should be minimized). Howbeit, the problem is that the state vector includes
the control inputs of the previous sampling instant. Thus, an extra state has to be included, qout3,2, which
actually represents the two time step delayed outﬂow of the third reach. With the help of this new state
the constraint, qout3,2(k) −qlat3(k −1) > 0, has been obtained which is equivalent to the aforementioned
desired constraint. Remember that the number after the comma in the subscripts indicates the delay time
steps. As a result, the new state, input rate of change, disturbance and controlled variables vectors can be
formulated as:

The state space matrices used for standard MPC and EMPC for this simulation scenario can be found in
==PAGE:146==

Appendix B.5. It is very important to underline the fact that zfull in not used from EMPC since there are no
reference values to be tracked.
Regarding standard MPC, the demands are represented by a reference, the deviation from which is heavily
penalized. It is certain that in order to satisfy all three lateral demands to the same extend as much as
possible, the same penalty should be used. The cost function is formulated as in Equation 6.8, subject to
3.69, 3.82 and 3.83 and the weight matrices have been selected to be:
Qz,full = diag{40, 40, 40, 10, 40, 5, 90, 90, 90, 0.002, 0.4}
Rfull = diag{1, 1, 1, 5, 5, 5, 5, 5, 5}
(7.15)
It is evident that the lateral offtakes are more heavily penalized so that they are satisﬁed as much as possible.
In addition, Lake 1 is more lightly penalized than the three reaches and Lake 2 because of the fact that it is
large and its purpose is to provide enough water to the river system. On the contrary, the penalization of
turbine and pump ﬂow is very small considering that they should be allowed to deviate from zero when it is
needed. Aside from that, the wasted ﬂow out of the river is moderately penalized for deviating from zero in
order to be minimized but without deteriorating the performance of the whole system. This means that the
system is allowed to extract the appropriate amount of water in order to meet its objectives (i.e. reference
tracking). In relation to Rfull, it is obvious that the rate of change of the ﬂows through the turbine, the
pump and the gates have been penalized more in order to avoid unnecessary stress of the equipment. Finally,
concerning the slack variables, the method described in Section 6.4.4 was applied with ypen = 1015.
On the other hand, for the case of EMPC, the control inputs representing the lateral outﬂows are constrained
to be zero when there are no demands. Oppositely, when demands are present, the lateral outﬂows are
constrained to the values of the demands. This way, EMPC provides water to the customers only when it is
requested . With appropriately chosen Ceco, in order to obtain the desired elements from the state vector, as
it can be seen in Appendix B.5, the cost function has been formulated to be:

subject to Equations 7.8 and 7.10. The vectors used in this cost function are formulated exactly in the same
fashion as in Equation 7.2 i.e. contain the prices, ﬂows, penalization of the rate of change of the control
variables, the slack variables and their penalization for all the time instants of the prediction horizon as
explained in Section 7.2. More speciﬁcally, the vectors qLat1, qLat2 and qLat3 represent the predictions of
the lateral outﬂows, qlat1, qlat2 and qlat3 for the whole prediction horizon respectively, whilst CL1, CL2

117
and CL3 represent their prices for the whole prediction horizon, respectively. Moreover, by observing the
formulated cost function of EMPC, it becomes apparent that the previously described method of handling
the constraints regarding the lateral offtakes results to the minimization of the cost function by providing as
much water as possible only when it is requested. Besides, the selected elements of Reco are:

while for Sneco, it was selected that ypen = 1015. Regarding the elements of Reco, it is indicative that the

The constraints of the system used in this scenario can be viewed in Table 7.4 while it is underlined that the
calculated control signals are applied on the nonlinear model. Note that the maximum and minimum rate
of change of the lateral outﬂows are very high because it is supposed that there is a group of customers in
each lateral outﬂow, thus this rate of change is the sum for all the customers. Prediction horizon is set to
Np = 70 = 19.4 hours. Therefore, in the simulation scenario created, there is a demand of 50 m3/s from all
three lateral outﬂows, qlat1, qlat2, qlat3 for approximately 46 hours, as Figure 7.12 suggests. This ﬁgure also
demonstrates how the lateral offtake demands of the three groups of customers are satisﬁed by each control
strategy. Note that there is a signiﬁcant lateral outﬂow price drop from 40 hours to 44 hours of the simulation
as depicted in Figure 7.13. In particular, even thought it is not clear in the plot, the third lateral outﬂow has
been selected to be slightly more expensive than the second which, in turn is slightly more expensive than
the ﬁrst one. This pattern results to a more equal satisfaction of lateral outﬂow demands of the different
groups of customers, regarding EMPC. Of course, again, these prices incorporate also other factors than just
economics. In addition, the same ﬁgure depicts the total amount of lateral offtakes as well as the ﬂow out of
the river, whose reference is not plotted as it is identical to the minimum constraint. It should be borne in
mind that plots that are not reported here can be found at Appendix C.4.
Coming now to the interpretation of the obtained results, ﬁrstly, it is apparent that before the drop of the
prices concerning the lateral outﬂows , EMPC fully satisﬁes the requests of the groups of customers 1 and 3
whereas group of customers 2 is partially satisﬁed. The reason can be seen in the prices of water for each
customer. To be more speciﬁc, EMPC prefers to satisfy group of customers 3 since the price for them is a bit
more expensive compared to the others. Moreover, for group of customers 1, it easy to deliver the requested
water as it is located closer to Lake 2 by using the turbine. By inspection of Figures 7.12, C.22 and C.23
it becomes obvious that in the beginning of the simulation there is enough water in the backwater area of
the second reach as well as in Lake 2 in order to both satisfy the demand of qlat1 and also to provide water
to the next reaches. Afterwards (roughly around 45 hours), since these water levels are approaching their
minimum constraints, less water is provided to qlat1. Especially for EMPC it can be seen that it is preferred
to satisfy more qlat2 and qlat3, thus the water ﬂowing from Lake 1 gets transferred directly to Reach 3, while
the ﬁrst group of customers gets satisﬁed mostly with the help of the turbine and the water that exists in the
backwater area of Reach 2, as can be observed in Figures C.24 and C.25. On the other hand, with standard
MPC a more equal distribution is pursued. In relation to EMPC, more equal distribution can be achieved, if
deviation of lateral requests from reference values is included in the cost function transforming the problem
from LP to QP. Howbeit, this would lead to a smaller exploitation of the changing prices.
More speciﬁcally, during lateral offtakes price drop, EMPC stops to satisfy the customers’ needs since it
foresees that it will be more advantageous, from an economic point of view, to deliver the water afterwards
==PAGE:148==
118
CHAPTER 7. EMPC FOR AN EXTENDED WATER SYSTEM
while the prices will rise again. To do so, EMPC increases the water levels at Reach 2 and Reach 3 (see
Figure C.23) during the period of low prices. The large difference with standard MPC here is that the latter
is not aware of these changes in the prices and as a consequence starts to gradually reduce the offtakes to the
customers. This has as a result that while both techniques provide approximately the same amount of water
(standard MPC is found to give 4% more water than EMPC regarding total lateral outﬂows ), EMPC exploits
better the time varying prices.
This simulation scenario’s goal was to illustrate how the two different formulations face an extreme case of
lateral outﬂow requests. It goes without saying that a straightforward comparison of the two methods is not
fair since each method tries to minimize a different cost function. To be more speciﬁc, standard MPC tries to
minimize the deviation of all the controlled variables from the reference values, even though these deviations
have been penalized lightly, while EMPC does not have to follow a reference vector. In addition, whilst for
EMPC lateral offtakes constitute constraints of the respective control variables, for standard MPC they just
represent reference values that should be followed (with a high penalization for their deviations). This can
explain the behaviour of the third lateral offtake at the end of the high demand period at roughly around
66 hours. In particular, while standard MPC changes the lateral offtake almost when the reference value
changes (since a deviation is allowed), EMPC does the same well in advance (note that qout3 cannot change
very violently) in order to guarantee that the lateral offtake will be zero when the demand stops (since it is a
constraint that has to be respected) and as a result to minimize the wasted outﬂow. This is not noticed for
qlat1 and qlat2 since they can get water directly from the backwater areas of the reaches they are located at.
One extra remark is that the results are highly dependent on the selected weights of the controllers. For
example, from Figure 7.13, it can be observed that implementation of standard MPC leads to higher wasted
outﬂow. However, if its deviation from zero had been penalized more less waste would be the case. Finally,
as far EMPC is concerned, the prices of turbine and pump ﬂow have been kept constant during the whole
simulation (see Figure C.30) and during the largest part of the simulation smaller than the price of the lateral
offtakes. Thereby, EMPC tries mainly to satisfy the customers’ requests and not to maximize the economic
proﬁt by using the turbine. As a matter of fact, a combination of Scenarios 1 and 2 would be closer to a
realistic case. However, the choice to have two separated scenarios helps to have a more clear view of the
core concept of EMPC.
Figure 7.12: Lateral offtakes for Scenario 2 of EMPC.

7.5
Conclusion
This chapter dealt with the formulation of EMPC for the extended water system treated in this thesis and
with the demonstration of its core concept. To do so, two simulation scenarios were executed. In particular,
the ﬁrst one concerned changes in the prices of the ﬂows through the pump and the turbine as well as the
ﬂow out of the river. It was shown that EMPC can take advantage of windows of low prices for the pump
ﬂow and the water outﬂow as well as to exploit the periods of high price for the turbine ﬂow. Unquestionably,
smaller penalization of the rate of change of the control inputs leads to better economic performance but
==PAGE:150==
120
CHAPTER 7. EMPC FOR AN EXTENDED WATER SYSTEM
with the price of a more intense use of the control signals whilst larger prediction horizon also brings more
fruitful exploitation of the low and high price windows but, inevitably, results to larger computation times.
Therefore, there is trade-off which should always be expected for EMPC which depends on the speciﬁcations
of each problem. The second simulation scenario regarded the delivery of water in order to satisfy certain
lateral outﬂow requests. A decisive step towards this directions was the transformation of lateral off-takes
from disturbances to control variables. Comparison of standard MPC and EMPC revealed the different way
these formulations face the problem where the most distinctive difference was the more efﬁcient way that
EMPC exploited the price changes.
==PAGE:151==
CHAPTER 8
Conclusion
8.1
Findings and Comments
At this thesis, the modelling and control of a river system was investigated. The targets of this project were
stated back in Section 1.5 and revising them, it can be said that:
• The ﬁrst target set for this thesis was to obtain an accurate but simple model for river systems. Initially,
Saint Venant equations, which are nonlinear PDEs and describe accurately the dynamics of water
systems, were examined. Chapter 2 presented ways to numerically integrate these equations and
afterwards Chapter 4 demonstrated the linearization and discretization procedure for a single reach
river system. Step and impulse tests veriﬁed the theoretical argumentation that each river reach consists
of a part of uniform ﬂow, where the water levels at the discretization points are almost identical, and
a part of backwater area where the water level gradually increases. The result was to end up with
an accurate but high order model with many states which is unsuitable for control purposes of large
water systems. Thereby, ID model was developed and special attention was given to the identiﬁcation
of backwater area and time delay since these parameters change substantially with different ﬂow
conditions. Open loop simulations showed that the behaviour of both models (discretized Saint Venant
equations and ID) agree with this of the nonlinear model. Small discrepancies can be seen mainly away
from the selected operating point. Afterwards, both models were used for control of the nonlinear
Saint Venant model and in Chapter 4 the obtained results seem to be almost identical. Thereby, ID
model proved to be compact and reliable enough in order to be used for control design later without
leading to large computational times.
• After the theoretical presentation of all the available control methods in Chapter 3, where LQR and
MPC were fully developed, Chapter 5 dealt with the implementation of these two main representatives
of the class of optimal controllers, for a single and a double reach river system. Although unconstrained
MPC without anticipation and standard LQR produced identical results, the used performance indexes
revealed the superiority of MPC with anticipation. The cause behind this is the capability of MPC to
incorporate in its formulation future reference and disturbance values and react in advance to handle
them better. When hard constraints are present, MPC can naturally include them during its formulation
whereas for LQR constraints can be implemented only as saturation values for the control inputs
but cannot be taken into account in its cost function. Simulations showed clearly that in this case,
the responses of these two control strategies differ a lot. Actually, LQR with saturation is not able
to handle efﬁciently disturbance changes and in the case of a huge disturbance change, ﬂoods can
121
==PAGE:152==
122
CHAPTER 8. CONCLUSION
easily happen since it is not aware of the existing constraints. Ultimately, after the implementation of
soft constrained MPC, it was showed that infeasibility is avoided when large disturbances and tight
constraints are present. Thereby, the target to prove the superiority of MPC compared to other control
methods was fulﬁlled.
• The next goal was to establish an extended water system. This was done in Chapter 6 were the model
of a water system with a river with four reaches, two lakes, four gates, a weir, a turbine and a pump
was created. However, after the appropriate formulation of the cost function, the problem of the
heavy computational load became apparent with this relatively large system. Since slack variables
are decision variables of MPC and have a considerable impact on the computational time, various
ways to handle them were tested. In particular, these strategies spanned from the use of different
slack variables for the next and each future time instant of the prediction horizon for each state to the
employment of a single slack variable for all the time instants in the prediction horizon for all the states.
In order to be able to evaluate the different methods reliably, six performance indexes were employed
in order to track accurately the behaviour of each strategy as well as to record the system resources it
uses. The simulations illustrated evidently the fact that smaller amount of slack variables means lighter
use of system resources (computational time and memory) but with considerably worse performance
when a violation of the slack variables was the case. Nevertheless, taking into consideration that
the majority of the cases concern scenarios where no violation of the soft constraints takes place
and thereby computational load is the only thing of interest, a method according to which one slack
variable is used for each softened state for the whole prediction horizon was chosen since it leads to
signiﬁcantly smaller computational times and memory usage without bringing unnecessarily large
violations of soft constraints.
• Last but not least, EMPC was formulated accordingly for a river system in Chapter 7 where the
differences in the design of standard MPC and EMPC became apparent. Theoretical development
of EMPC was done back in Chapter 3. Furthermore, with the aid of a properly designed simulation
scenario, different modes of operation of the extended water system were investigated and consequently
the core concept behind EMPC became even more clear. In addition, it was demonstrated that a trade
off should always be expected between economic performance and wear of equipment as well as
between economic performance and computation time. The next step was to accordingly alter the
formulation of standard and economic MPC in order to convert the lateral off-takes of the customers
from known disturbances to decision variables. With the aid of an appropriate simulation, the different
way standard and economic MPC face the demands of customers was revealed. Conclusively, it can
be stated that the development of EMPC constitutes a decisive step towards the wiser exploitation of
water resources.
8.2
Future work
Indisputably, numerous interesting and motivating directions of further research result from the above work.
More speciﬁcally:
• As it has already been underlined, this thesis does not deal with modelling of gates. To be more
speciﬁc, the controller produces the desired ﬂow and then it is assumed that a local PID controller
generates the appropriate movement of the gates. Therefore, it would be of great interest to ﬁnd
efﬁcient ways to model the gates. As reported in [10] when linearised models of gates are used then
these approximations deteriorate greatly, away from the selected linearization points. The suggestion
==PAGE:153==
8.2. FUTURE WORK
123
by the author regarding the use of a nonlinear model of the gates in combination with discretized Saint
Venant equations could be tested for the concept of this project.
• Unquestionably, the ID model offers low computational burden but describes the water levels and
discharge only at one point located at the end of each reach. It is easy to understand that for reaches
with no regular ﬂood levels, this is not the best choice since the avoidance of ﬂoods for the modelled
point does not necessarily ensure that ﬂoods are avoided for the whole reach. Therefore, before
applying the designed controller to a real river system, tests and evaluation should be made based on
a full hydrodynamic model with a dense grid in order to model a sufﬁciently high number of water
levels and discharges.
• In the simulation scenarios presented in this thesis, the disturbances were assumed to be step changes.
Practically speaking, this is not realistic. Thus, a bigger class of inputs should be tested such as
sinusoidal and ramp signals.
• For this thesis, it was assumed that every controlled water level can be measured at each sampling
instant. Moreover, since the model is assumed to have small uncertainty and the disturbances are
assumed to be perfectly known, the rest of the states can be calculated through straightforward
manipulations as they are delayed versions of either the disturbances or the control inputs. Certainly,
this may not be the case for many river systems. Therefore, it would be helpful to develop a Kalman
ﬁlter to estimate the unmeasured states and then evaluate the performance of the developed control
strategy.
• As a matter of fact, the assumption that future disturbances are perfectly known is not realistic
since for example weather predictions are never 100% accurate or some customers may take an
amount of water without earlier notice. Additionally, the larger the horizon of the prediction for these
disturbances, the higher the probability of an inaccurate prediction. Thereby, a robustness check for
the designed controller against disturbance uncertainty is important. Obviously, noise could be added
to the disturbance prediction and as proposed in [54] and [21], Multiple Model Predictive Control
scheme could be developed in order to take into account predictions with different probabilities of
existence. Moreover, EMPC can use Second Order Cone Programming (SOCP) in order to handle
uncertainties both in model and prediction. While this technique is deteriorating the performance in
the nominal case, it can avoid a solution which would be very expensive because of the uncertainty.
Details can be seen in [33]. Additionally, robustness regarding plant-model mismatch would be an
interesting aspect to investigate further.
• As mentioned in Section 2.5, various factors like evaporation and ground water were not taken into
account during the modelling process. It goes without saying that an interesting expansion would be
to model also these parameters in order to obtain an even more accurate model.
• As stated in Section 1.4 decentralized and centralized control schemes can be applied in a typical
water system like the one treated in this thesis. The controlled system designed and tested in Chapter
6 was a centralized one since the whole system was regulated by one main controller. Undeniably,
the development of decentralized controllers using distributed control architecture for the different
subsystems of the full water system and comparison with the centralized one would reveal interesting
ﬁndings.
• Real-time applications need fast computation of the appropriate control input. As a matter of fact,
tailored algorithms exist that can actually speed up by several times the the conducted optimization
==PAGE:154==
124
CHAPTER 8. CONCLUSION
with the price of a suboptimal solution, though. Dantiz-Wolfe decomposition algorithm is a tailored
algorithm which is suitable for EMPC control and with early termination technique can reduce the
time of computation as in [68].
• Another way in order to reduce the computation time of the optimization of MPC, is to use smaller
control horizon than prediction horizon; that is, Nu < Np. In this case the decision variables are less.
This can be applied if it is assumed that the reference tracking will be achieved after a number of
iterations smaller than the prediction horizon and thus the input will not need to change. The general
trend is that the smaller Nu is, the less optimal is the obtained solution. Moreover, special attention
should be given when Nu is selected to be much smaller than Np since the obtained behaviour may be
far from the expected one. More details about the choice of prediction and control horizon can be
found in [59].
• By investigating Appendix B and more speciﬁcally the matrices of the established state space models,
the sparsity of these matrices becomes evident. Computational efﬁcient methods can be developed in
order to exploit this feature. An interesting approach can be found in [50].
• Turbine and pump dynamics have been neglected during this thesis since it was assumed that the
produced and absorbed power is directly proportional to the ﬂow through them. In order to generate
hydropower in a realistic manner during the simulations, these dynamics have to be taken into
consideration in detail during the modelling procedure of the extended water system. The basic
guidelines which can be used as a solid starting point can be found in [46].

A.1
Discretization Procedure for a LTI State Space Model
Nowadays, control is conducted using computers and thus discrete time models are needed. As explained in
[30], the discrete time model can be obtained by exploiting the solution of the LTI state space model:
˙x(t) = Ax(t) + Bu(t)
y(t) = Cx(t) + Du(t)
(A.1)
which is:
x(t) = eAtx0 + eAt
Z t

e−AtBu(τ)dτ
(A.2)
For the sampling instants t1 = kTs and t2 = (k + 1)Ts Equation A.2 becomes, respectively:
x(kTs) = eAkTsx0 + eAkTs

(A.3)

APPENDIX A. BACKGROUND MATERIAL
A.2
Pade Approximation of a Time Delay
As explained in Chapter 6 there is a need to represent a time delay while performing the non-linear
simulations regarding the extended water system. As a matter of fact, as stated in [75], the majority of
transport processes present a dead-time that has to be taken into consideration in order to obtain accurate
models. The problem is that the closed loop system including dead time is an inﬁnite dimensional system.
Henceforth, approximations are requested in order to model the dead time and analyse the system. The step
response of a dead time is simply a delayed step-signal:
h(t) = r(t −T)
(A.7)
where T represents the dead time. The Laplace transformation of this step response is:
H(s) = e−T s
(A.8)
Probably, the most well-known approximation for time delay is the Pade-approximation which can be
expressed as:
e−T s ≈1 −k1s + k2s2 + . . . ± knsn
1 + k1s + k2s2 + . . . + knsn
(A.9)
where n is the order of approximation and coefﬁcients ki are functions of the dead time. For instance, if
second order approximation is selected then the coefﬁcients are:
k1 = T
2 ,
k2 = T 2
12 ,
ki = 0 for other i.
(A.10)
Deﬁnitely, the higher the order is the more accurate is the approximation leading to heavier computational
loads. Furthermore, the step response shows a sharp jump at t = 0 s since the numerator and denominator
have the same degree [75]. Therefore, at t = 0 s there is an output signal instead of delaying the input.
The aforementioned drawback will become clearer with an example. So, if it assumed that the dead time is
T = 5 s, Figure A.1 depicts the step response for second, ﬁfth and tenth order Pade approximation together
with the exact time delay. Note that Pade function of Matlab ® is used in order to produce the results.
The conclusion that can be made is that although, especially for low order approximations, the results do not
follow closely the behaviour of the exact time delay, Pade approximation offers a reliable way to include a
dead time in the performed non-liner simulations. Howbeit, if a more accurate approximation is needed and
the initial jump should be avoided, the solution is to use Pade approximation where the numerator’s degree
is one less than that of the denominator as reported in [75].
==PAGE:159==
A.3. CONVEX OPTIMIZATION PROBLEMS
129
Figure A.1: Second, ﬁfth and tenth order Pade approximation of a time delay of 5 seconds.
A.3
Convex Optimization Problems
The analysis that follows is mainly inspired by [70]. A typical optimization problem has the following form:
minimize: f0(x)
subject to: fi(x) ≤bi, i = 1, . . . , m
(A.11)
where x ∈Rn is the optimization variable, function f0 : R →R is the objective function and the functions
fi : R →R constitute the inequality constraint functions. In addition, constants b1 . . . bm are called
constraints or bounds. A vector x∗is called optimal when it is the solution of the problem. This means that
it gives the smallest value for the objective value whereas it satisﬁes all the existing constraints.
Usually, the optimization problems include objective function and inequality constraint functions of a speciﬁc
form. The most simple one is the linear form which implies that:
fi(αx + βy) = αfi(x) + βfi(y)
(A.12)
∀x ∈Rn, y ∈Rn, α ∈R and β ∈R. Furthermore, for convex optimization problems it holds true that:
fi(αx + βy) ≤αfi(x) + βfi(y)
(A.13)
∀x ∈Rn, y ∈Rn and α ∈R, β ∈R with α + β = 1 and αβ ≥0 . Apparently, convexity is a more
general property than linearity. If the objective function to be minimized is convex and so do the constraint
inequalities then a convex optimization problem is obtained whose feasible region is a convex set. To be
more speciﬁc, a set C is convex if the line segment between any two points in C lies in C. For example, if
x1 and x2 ∈X and 0 ≤φ ≤1, it is true that:
φx1 + (1 −φ)x2 ∈C
(A.14)
==PAGE:160==
130
APPENDIX A. BACKGROUND MATERIAL
if C is a convex set. Figure A.2 includes examples of convex and non convex sets. Evidently, the medium
set is not convex as the line that connects the two points in not entirely inside the set while the right set is not
convex since it does not include all the boundary points.
Figure A.2: One convex and two non convex sets [70].
The typical quadratic problem, which are often encountered when dealing with MPC, are of the
form:
minimize: xT Hox + cT x
subject to: Ax −b = 0 and Cx −D ≤0
(A.15)
with Ho ∈Rn×n, c ∈Rn, A ∈Rp×n, b ∈Rp, C ∈Rq×n, d ∈Rq is said to be convex [52]
when the Hessian matrix Ho is positive semi-deﬁnite (xT Hox ≥for all non-zero vectors with real
entries, i.e. ∀x ∈Rn\{0}). If Ho is positive deﬁnite then the quadratic problem is strictly convex.
Convex problems have only one globally optimal solution whilst non convex sets may present multiple locally
optimal solutions. Generally speaking, there is no analytic formula for the solution of convex problems but
with the aid of very effective methods, like the interior-point method that is used for this thesis, an accurate
solution can be obtained within a relatively small number of iterations. On the other hand, when a non
convex problem is faced, it requests an amount of time exponential to the number of states and constraints
in order to return either infeasibility or a globally optimal solution, if it exists. In addition, interior point
method is a suitable choice as it can treat efﬁciently linear, quadratic, conic and smooth non-linear functions.
Therefore, interior point method is reported to solve quickly and robustly even large problems with tight
constraints.
A.4
Stability of MPC
This following analysis is mainly inspired by [43]. Since MPC is actually a feedback control method, there
is a risk that the obtained closed-loop system may be unstable. To make it more clear, each sampling
instant the performance is optimized over the prediction horizon but without caring what happens beyond
this. Therefore, it is possible that MPC drives the system into a state that will be impossible to stabilize.
Undeniably, the tighter are the constraints of the optimization problem the more likely is the aforementioned
situation. It may be easy to guess that a short prediction horizon can also increase the probability of instability.
Hence, usually a long prediction horizon or even inﬁnite can guarantee stability.
==PAGE:161==
A.4. STABILITY OF MPC
131
Another way to ensure stability is by adding some terminal constraints, x(Np) = 0. In particular, this term
forces the state vector to have this speciﬁc value at the end of the prediction horizon. Of course, it does not
mean that the state vector will actually be at zero after Np samples. This is summarized by the following:
Let the plant:
x(k + 1) = f(x(k), u(k))
be optimized by minimizing the cost function:
V (k) =
Np
X
i=1
l(x(k + i|k), u(k + i −1|k))
where l(x, u) ≥0 and l(x, y) = 0 only if x = 0 and u = 0. Moreover, l is decrescent and subject
to:
x(Np) = 0
As it is well known, optimization is conducted with respect to u subject to the constraints x ∈X
and u ∈U where X,U are some sets. If (x, u) = 0 is an equilibrium condition for the plant
and the receding horizon tactic is employed then this equilibrium point is stable and therefore the
optimization is feasible and can be solved at each time step.
Proof of this theorem can be found in [43] where the cost function is used as Lyapunov function. The
challenge with this approach is that it is assumed that the globally optimal solution can be found at each time
step; something that is not always easy. Hence, a frequent relaxation of the inclusion of terminal constraints
is instead of using just a point, to use a terminal constraint set X0 that contains the origin . The concept here
is to drive the system with MPC to this constraint set and afterwards to use a (conventional) control law that
ensures the stabilization of the system for initial conditions inside set X0. In addition, it is supposed that all
the existing constraints of the system are inactive inside the set X0 so that the control law (the conventional
one) is able to lead the system to the origin. This type of schemes that ﬁrstly use a predictive control and then
employ another control law are usually called dual mode predictive control schemes. Finally, another way to
ensure closed loop stability of MPC is to use a predeﬁned factor in order to contract the state vector [83]:
∥x(k + 1|k)∥≤α∥x(k)∥
(A.16)
with α < 1. This method is usually referred to as contraction constraint.
==PAGE:162==
==PAGE:163==

B.4. STATE SPACE SYSTEM OF THE EXTENDED WATER SYSTEM FOR SCENARIO 1 OF EMPC
139
B.4
State Space System of the Extended Water System for Scenario 1 of EMPC
The established matrices regarding state space model of the extended water system after the expansion
analysed in Section 3.4.3 and following the notation that the number after the comma reveals the number of
delay time steps are shown in the next pages. Note that here lateral off-takes are considered disturbance
variables and that this state space system was used with the ﬁrst scenario regarding the evaluation of EMPC.
Certainly, there is no matrix Czd,full since EMPC has no reference values to follow.

B.5. STATE SPACE SYSTEM OF THE EXTENDED WATER SYSTEM FOR SCENARIO 2 OF EMPC
143
B.5
State Space System of the Extended Water System for Scenario 2 of EMPC
The established matrices regarding state space model of the extended water system after the expansion
analysed in Section 3.4.3 and following the notation that the number after the comma reveals the number of
delay time steps are shown in the next pages. Note that here lateral off-takes are considered control variables
and that this state space system was used with the second scenario regarding the evaluation of EMPC for
EMPC and standard MPC. It is important to see that matrix Ceco is used only for EMPC while Czd,full is
used only for standard MPC since EMPC has no reference values to track.

C.1
Additional Plots for Slack Variables Handling Methods Comparison,
Scenario 1 of Standard MPC
At this section, additional plots regarding the ﬁrst scenario (violation of soft constraints) used for evaluation
of different methods of handling slack variables for standard MPC, are presented. Note that at most cases
all the methods produce identical results and therefore it is almost impossible to distinguish the individual
responses.
